nog 13s 6d in payment of the casli va ue of the Sonus for 1091, the
Policy being for 10,000, and insured in 1836, on the live year distribution plan. 11ns

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 19, 1801.
Bogus Banks.
The closing months of 1891 promise favour-
ably to see the death and burial of most of
the bogus banks of Australia. Within the
last few years these institutions have broken
out like an epidemic in all parts of this con-
tinent, but more particularly in New South
Wales. They mostly arose suddenly and
without warning. In the evening there was
only a void, and in the morning there was a
full-grown bank, with an enormous paid-up
capital, and a ponderous reserve-fund, and a
large sum to the credit of profit and loss
account. There w r as an empty office to-day,
and to-morrow there was a glaring pro-
spectus, also a table and three cane chairs, a
Hebrew with an overhanging beak, a pot of
ink, and an advertisement, and out of these
materials the private bank was constructed.
Some of these establishments had a more or
less remote connection with time-payment
furniture and oleographs that are traded off
on the instalment plan ; some of them also
dealt in bills of sale and loans which are
secured by a mortgage on the bedstead ; and
they all had a long list of directors who
struggled desperately to lend a solid, solvent,
affluent expression to their names by affixing
“Esq.” to the bald and naked “John
Hogg” with which nature had endowed
them. Now and then some especially thick-
headed and impecunious legislator appeared
on the directorate, but there was a second-
hand aspect about tire board and the entire
office—an unspeakable and indescribably
subdued air of back lanes and the
three-card trick, which prevented the new
institutions establishing an influential con-
nection in monetary circles. The staff’s eyes
were too close together, and it had too
much oil on its hair and bogus jewellery
on its claws, and it grabbed too hungrily
after the deposits, and it was too ready
to offer any interest under the sun so long
as it could trade off its deposit-receipts for
hard cash, and, taking it all round, the
“ bank ” was or ought to have been a trans-
parent fraud from the baseless assertion on
the front-door to t he ash-barrel in the yard
—from the chairman of the board down to
the office-boy—a fraud in its advertisement
and its prospectus and. its balance-sheet—in
its general manager and its auditors—in the
howling lie painted on the plate-glass w indow,
and in the blatant untruth on its note-paper,
and the soul-destroying perjury on its official
seal and its gigantic brass-plate. In short,
the average Australian private bank started
as an imposture at the beginning, and will
remain one till it bursts, and in all its deal-
ings there is no good in it.
* * *
There are certain infallible signs by which
the bogus banks can be invariably distin-
guished from those other institutions which
were built to last, instead of being merely
built to burst. In the first place, none, or
hardly any, of them had any visible begin-
ning. They appeared in a full-grown state,
without notice or any apparent process
of formation. Even in a country where
money is much more plentiful than in Aus-
tralia, the organisation of a great financial
institution is a work of time. It is necessary
to get the capital together, and the list of
shareholders is generally made up of the
names of well-known capitalists. But the
majority of the now “banks” of recent
years went through no preliminary stage
whatever, so far as the public eye could
discern, and the £200,000 or so of paid-up
capital was subscribed mostly by uncertifi-
cated insolvents, tenth-rate pawnbrokers,
cheap and gaudy clerks, and miscellaneous
individuals in nnpaid-for clothes, who had
formerly lived, with no visible means of
support, as commission - agents with no
commissions, or as money-lenders with no
money to lend. They were shifty-looking per-
sons with no furniture, no banking accounts,
and no assets ; some of them had been in
gaol, some of them were endowed with
aliases, a good many had been co
respondents, and almost all were otherwise
shady. All combined they could not reason-
ably be suspected of even the semblance of
affluence, and the assertion that they had
put up the alleged capital of even the
smallest bank that ever existed, without
turning a hair, was a palpable absurdity.
The manager of the institution was
generally tit and appropriate company for
the founders, inasmuch as he seldom looked
like a man whom any sane board would
leave in an unchained condition in the
vicinity of any capital—paid-up or otherwise.
The board, as already mentioned, consisted
mostly of certain obscure persons, all of
whom were denominated “Esq.” Half of
them gave no addresses, and the other half
hailed from localities where capital is
notoriously scarce. And, finally, the auditors
were almost invariably some now individuals
who had just opened a small office in a back
street, and who had no reputation to lose if
they attested and swore to the solvency of
everything and everybody in one act.
* # *
The bogus bank, having got over its preli-
minary stage, and emerged into the present
tense, may also be known by the manner in
which it pays lavish dividends and hungers
after deposits—deposits that are fixed for
three years, in particular--at any price.
It professes to make its income by discount-
ing bills, granting overdrafts, and lending
out money on mortgage, and in all these
branches of industry it is almost impos-
sible to realise more than 7 per cent, all
round on a steady average. The legitimate
bank, therefore, seldom pays anything like
a respectable dividend for the first few years
of its existence. It lends out its * own
capital; it also receives immense sums on
current account without interest, and lends
a considerable portion of them the
returns therefrom being clear profit ;
it prints notes and lends them at
interest—-another item which is mostly pro-
fit ; it pays small rates for deposits”, and
makes advances at high rates with the pro-
ceeds ; it gains a small but never-failing in-
come from the accidental destruction of
notes which go out in exchange for hard cash
and never comeback ; and even with all these
and many other sources of income its pro-
gress is generally slow. It is only when a
large reserve fund has been accumulated that
the genuine bank begins to pay large divi-
dends ; and even then in a period of great
depression it often gets into serious diffi-
culties. The National Bank of New Zealand,
as a fair example, has written off more than
two-thirds of its capital in losses resulting,
not so much from bad management, as from
hopeless stagnation; and all the profits of what
is commonly termed “ legitimate banking”
have proved insufficient to make business
profitable. But the bogus bank—a term
which probably covers three-fourths of the
institutions which have sprung up of recent
years—has h irdly any of these advantages.
It has very little paid-up capital to lend—of
that fact the long lists of notoriously im-
pecunious directors is a sufficient guarantee.
It makes little or no profit out of current
accounts, for it pays interest on them, usually
at a liberal rate. It has no note issue, and
thus a most valuable source of revenue is cut
off; and it pays tremendous rates of interest
for deposits, which is the one never-failing
sign that disasteivmust come to somebody—
or everybody—at some period in the near
future. Also, though its visible income is
infinitesimal and precarious it almost in-
variably pays larger dividends from the very
beginning than does the legitimate bank
with all its multifarious sources of profit.
* * #
The piratical financial institution lives, in
fact, mostly by taking in deposits and lend-
ing out the money of depositors, and in most
cases it pays for these deposits about two per
cent, more than it can possibly hope to
realise even in the most profitable branch of
business. The working expenses of the
establishment, the rents, the lavish salaries,
and the bad debts are all unprovided for, and
there is a dead loss besides. Therefore the
dubious bank always offers its most lurid
terms for deposits that are “fixed for three
years,” that it may have time to burst
decently, or to bolt, or to pass on the
responsibility, before the day of repayment
arrives. And in many cases it even offers
an additional inducement of one or two per
cent, on top of the original nine or ten,
provided the deposit is renewed for a further
term, which is an open and unvarnished
acknowledgment that the institution desires,
at any cost, to avoid repayment as long as
possible. The current rate of interest at
that future period may be only 4 3 per cent.,
and the banker who joyously pledges himself
to pay 10 or 12, regardless of all chances or
contingencies, is obviously a person who
would undertake anything, if the date of the
undertaking were only far enough ahead.
Occasionally, too, he announces in one and
the same advertisement that ho wants to
borrow money from the public at 10 per
cent., and is willing to lend money to the
public on mortgage at six—a discrepancy
which ought to give his scheme away even in
a lunatic asylum, for under this system any
man could lodge his money with the com-
pany and borrow the same money back at a
reduction of four per cent., and thus get a
gratis annuity out of the institution for
life. And the establishment which professes
to pay lavish dividends by giving away per-
petual annuities to everybody is a vain thing
and of no account,
* * 9
Another of the reliable brands and ear-
marks of the fraudulent bank is the land
which it invariably has for sale cheap, on
longer and easier terms than were ever
offered by any other institution. The deposit
is small, and the interest is generally about
5 per cent., although the property was
ostensibly bought with money borrowed from
the public at nearly twice that figure, and
the establishment almost objects to cash,
and would rather sell on slow instalments
than otherwise. And the reason of this is
plain and easy of comprehension, for the
land is mortgaged to the last extremity, and
the instalments, as they come in, are swal-
lowed up in salaries and interest, or in
staving off rampant creditors who decline
to renew ; and as the company cannot convey
the land to the purchaser when his last pay-
ment is made, it has a preference for long
terms, and would gladly fix the land-buyer
for three years or more, even as it fixes the
depositor. The ultimate breakage is inevit-
able, but if the mass of the deposits fall due
just about the time when the bulk of the
land transactions are approaching comple-
tion, then the establishment can collapse or
bolt with a light heart, and a joyous con-
sciousness that it has scooped-the last avail-
able cent from everybody concerned,
# * *
The fraudulent bank is the only institution
of its kind which ever inserts the goodwill
of its business as a valuable asset in its bal-
ance-sheet. It is the only institution which
professes to have commenced business by
buying up the valuable connection of some
impoverished individual who owned nothing
but a small office, and paying him a large
sum in cash as an equivalent ; and when this
is done it may be safely assumed that there
was no business to buy, and no capital to
buy it with, and that the sum in question
was stolen out of the deposits and divided
among the directors. It is also the only
establishment whose balance-sheet shows a
record of absolutely unfailing prosperity.
The genuine bank, however successful, has
its years of depression and loss, but the
murky concern, which offers such surprising
terms for deposits, always announces a
lavish profit, no matter how ruinous the
times may be. It may be known also by the
fact that the profit is always represented by
the increase in the two items, “Advances ”
and “ Freehold Property,’' In other banks
these items often decline, while the item
“cash on hand” increases; but in the
bogus institution the supply of actual coin is
always limited, and the whole profits are con-
centrated under the two headings above-
mentioned. This facts looks all the more
surprising at a time like the present, when
freehold property is falling in value daily,
and it is difficult to find good securities
on which to make advances; but the ex-
planation is sufficiently easy. The advances
increase, because the directors and the
manager, and the manager’s uncle and the
directors’ brothers, have all taken large
overdrafts out of the depositors’ money, and
“ blewed ” the proceeds on horses, or
jewellery, or wild-cat mines, or ladies
with golden hair, and as the money
will never be repaid, they charge them-
selves 40 per cent, interest with a light
heart, and the interest keeps the asset called
“ advances ” piling up in the balance-sheet,
and the balance-sheet brings more deposits,
and out of the deposits more dividends are
paid and more advances made, and all is joy
and serenity. And the “freehold property ”
also mounts up, because the company bought
land to the value of £lO,OOO, on which £9OOO
is still owing ; and collected £4OOO in
instalments from credulous purchasers, and
the land is now worth only £6OOO in
a falling market, notwithstanding which fact,
the complaisant valuator has given his certi-
ficate that the property is now good and
sufficient value for £13,000. And because
of the surprising prosperity manifested under
these two headings, the “ goodwill” lias also
been written up 30 per cent,, and thus a
glorious future is assured for everybody
concerned.
» * «?
The bogus bank might possibly eke out a
precarious, hand-to-mouth existence on the
summitof a land-boom, but in times of depres-
sion, or even in times of moderate prosperity,
its case is absolutely hopeless. Therefore,
it becomes a mere sepulchre for lost deposits,
and a burial-place for dead shareholders,
and makes hardly a pretence of solvency.
Its one unfailing feature is its ceaseless
hunger after deposits. Other banks at times
almost hesitate to receive these, when profit-
able outlets are scarce, and they are paying
interest on money which is lying idle in their
vaults, but the bogus bank clamours for them
at all times and on almost any terms. It,
will cheerfully undertake to pay nine or ten
per cent, to depositors, and ton per cent,
more to shareholders, and will offer at the
same time to lend money to the public at
five or six, quite regardless of the fact that
it thereby loses some 15 per cent, in addition
to Avorking expenses. It never does lend
the money to the public, of course, for some
of it goes to stave off impatient creditors
and the rest is scrambled for by the directors
and the staff; and it is regardless of the 15
per cent, loss—it would lose 65 per cent,
with equal calmness. Its books are chaos •
its balance-sheet is constructed by putting
down any figures that will slaoav a profit of
45 per cent. ; its officials drift into a state of
maudlin recklessness in Avhich they collarthc
money almost openly as it comes in, and buy
jewellery with it; and its paid-up capital and
reserve-funds are betAveen £300,000 and
£400,000. And then some day a creditor
avlio Avon t be staved off presents a claim for
£lOO, and after that there is nothing but
general smash and emptiness. Sooner or
later they mostly go the same road. The
bank whose one ambition is to get
deposits fixed for three years on any
terms is utterly and absolutely hopeless,
and even Peruvian bonds or the repudiated
debentures of Honduras are more promising
than its securities. And then the liquida-
tion comes on as the final act, and all is over.
Nobody sends the directors and manager into
penal servitude, for there is a doubt in the
alleged mind of the court whether the case was
one of fraud or mere negligence. Nobody
gaols the auditor avlio swore to the company’s
prosperity, though he was obviously either a
gross swindler or a person who obtained
money under false pretences, inasmuch as he
pretended to be an auditor and yet couldn’t
or didn’t audit. The only individual who
comes to grief is the junior clerk who got
demoralised and took a hand in the general
scramble at the last moment, and bought a
bangle for a barmaid with the proceeds.
And the luav always drops on him like
billions of bricks, for the code of this Chris-
tian land is built that Avay.
Woman's Work.
The Sydney Trades andi ILabour Council (loot
an important step when it decided upon the
admission of delegates from; women’s associa-
tions. But it did not amount f!o> the recognition
of any new or revolutionary principle.. The
earnest advocates of whatever it is that may be
implied by the comprehensive' and hazy term
“ woman’s rights ” are in error in supposing
that the Labour Council thereby gave the
weight of their approval to the incursion ®f
women into the labour market as competitors,
Avith men. That question remains just where
it was before. The real meaning of the action
of the executiA 7 e of associated labour is this:
Recognising the fact that the labour market in
many departments is largely supplied from the
ranks of the female sex, they see that it is
better, in the interests not of Avomeu only, but
of labour as a whole, that these workers should
be organised and in touch with the general body
of workers. If it be a good thing to have large:
numbers of women in the army of wage-earners,,
then the benefit will be made all the more pro-
nounced by haA-ing them properly brigaded and
taught the regulation drill. If it be a bad.filling,,
then this course will minimise the evil. The
affiliation with women who are actually Avage-
earners is a measure of self-protection for the
male workers, and it leaves the desirability of
having them as wage-earners an open question*
** # v
And it is a serious question. It is a question
that is not in any way settled by asserting the
fundamental equality of the sexes, or by the
repeated sneer at the chivalry of the man who,
in the struggle for existence, pushes his weaker
sister aside. ■ The problem is a bigger one than
at first sight it looks. It is more than the
Avoman » problem ; it is the race - problem.
Whether woman’s true sphere is in the home or
in the factory is, Avhile things stand in their
present position, a triviality only fit for dis-
cussion in a debating society. Whatever her
“sphere” may be, a woman has the same
natural right as a man to engage in the occupa-
tion she finds best suited to her tastes and
Avants, let it be Avhat it may. If her inclina-
tions lead her to boiler-making, then, as an
abstract matter of right, no one is entitled to
step in and throw difficulties in the Avay of her
making boilers. But the practical question is
not what is right in the abstract, but what is
expedient in actually existing circumstances.
In every quarter of the civilised Avorld
to-day we see men fighting for the mere
chance to labour, in the hope that if they
succeed in getting the chance, they and their
wives and families may attain to something like
the measure of comfort enjoyed by a badly-
treated cab-horse; and knowing that if they fail,
it spells starvation. That is now the normal
condition of millions of our fellow-creatures on
the face of the globe. It is the logical and
inevitable outcome of modern social and
industrial conditions. In. face of such a state
of things, all talk of abstract rights is mere
babble. Will the woman wdio goes boiler-
making intensify that state of things, or will
she help to mend it ? If she adds one jot to the
bitterness of the struggle, all the abstract right
in the Avorld Avill not alter the fact that her
influence is for evil,
# * #
The two watchwords of civilisation in its
present stage are Monopoly and Competition.
Monopoly places in the hands of one class all
the natural factors of production, and drives
the other class into competition for the privilege
of access to them. Wages are ultimately
regulated by competition. When competition
is unrestricted, the well-known “iron law
brings wages doAvn to the loAVcst point at which


wage-earner and those dependent on him
tlie x jgt. That is to say, the wages in any
ca Lv.nnitV will depend entirely upon the
con „ e size of the family and the standard of
aVel fort or discomfort —below which it is
Possible for that particular community to
TLml Suppose the average family to con-
/ t of four individuals, and the lowest possible
BIS on which they can live to be half-a-
Jlwn a-head per week, then under un-
' Hl j,.fed competition wages will inevitably
I to ten 1 shillings a week. If the
irerage size of the family be reduced, then
f hfl tendency of the wages will be to come
wn in proportion. Thus, if the number of
0 remaining unmarried increases until the
average family—that is to say, the number of
individuals represented by each worker the
Linker of mouths fed by each pair of hands—
p reduced to three, then the rate of wages will
, jto 7s. 6d. a week. In other words, every
Lir of hands will, under unrestricted competi-
Hon earn just enough to feed the number of
Laths that on an average each pair of hands
“ („ f ee a. Mouths, not hands, determine the
•ate of wages. If, in a community of 1000, 250
' wage-earners, their combined earnings will,
miwhly speaking, just suffice to keep 1000
months from starvation. If, then, 250 more
mirs of hands are pressed into the service, the
result will be, not that the combined earnings
will he twice as much, but that wages will fall
till the gross receipts represent as before, the
bare subsistence of 1000 mouths.
* * *
Such, in outline, is the iron law of wages,
where ’ competition is unchecked. Trades-
unionism has supplied a check, but in the
nature of things, as the workers are now
beginning to see, it can only be a temporary
one. If the fortress of monopoly remains un-
shaken, trades-unionism must inevitably, in the
L go down before the growing pressure of
Free Competition. And it is beyond question
that the presence of women in the labour-market
must intensify the pressure. If the women are
isolated, unattached, independent units, without
husbands or brothers, or lovers, to contribute to
their support, then their influence on the
labour-market is exactly that of so many un-
married men, with just so much difference as
arises from the lower wage which they are con-
tent to accept. But when the wage-earning
women are members of a family, they represent
a danger which always threatens to become a
disaster. So long as it is only in scattered
cases that the wife or daughter helps by her
earnings to eke out the wages of the head of
the family, it may possibly be that in these
cases the family is in a slightly more comfort-
able position than one dependent entirely on the
earnings of a single pair of hands. Taking the
previous hypothesis of 10s. as the lowest
weekly income on which the average family of
four can exist, then of two equal families, one
getting 10s. from the work of the father, and
the other getting 15s. from the combined work
of father and mother, the latter family is on
the face of it better off. It has a weekly
margin of five shillings. It can store up for a
rainy day. It can indulge visions of entering
one day into the ranks of the capitalists.
* # *
But let this system of wage-earning wives
cease to be the exception, and become the rule,
and the iron-law comes crushing down in its
irresistible, relentless might. Once the average
pair of working hands comes to earn on an
average more than is absolutely necessary for
the number of mouths it has to the margin
will inevitably be pared away by Free Compe-
tition. Where once the man earned 10s. for
his family of four, eventually the man and
woman together will be able to earn only 10s.
This we see demonstrated in Maoriland, where,
under the woman-compositor system, two com-
positors, man and wife, earn together very
little, if at all, more than one male union com-
positor earns in Australia. The law is
inexorable. Mouths, not hands, determine the
rate of wages. Woman’s work, whatever it
maybe for the individual woman, means for the
race of workers as a whole increased competi-
tion, increased power to the oppression of
monopoly. If monopoly is still to reign, thus
everything that increases competition amongst
the workers is an evil. There are at present
two divergent tracks which seem to lead to the
ultimate destruction of monopoly. Labour
may use a ballot-box and the forms of constitu-
tional legislation intelligently, and do it in that
way. Or the race of unrestricted competition
may be allowed to go on, until the pressure on
the toiling bottom strata of humanity becomes
intolerable, and the pent-up forces break out
in a wild, bloody, shattering upheaval, spread-
ing ruin and universal devastation. If this
last method is to be accepted as embodying the
only hope for mankind, then the sooner the
catastrophe is precipitated the better, and
every form of headlong competition, including
the employment of women and children as com -
petitors with men in the world’s labour market,
should be encouraged. But if any other solu-
tion of the great problem is open, then the
onus of proof rests on those who put it forward
as a desirable thing that women should exercise
her undoubted natural right to labour side by
side with man.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Pauper Franchise.
Ihe visual Conservative person levels this
conundrum at the Sydney Telegraph
h i am to keep step with the times I must accept the
yveed that men are no longer the architects of their own
fortunes, and that there is no appreciable difference
letween the thousands of intelligent working-men who,
■ v industry, thrift, and self-denial, practised through
many years, have made homes for themselves and their
a muies, and the lazy, improvident, self-indulgent crowd
M the result of guzzling, gobbling and gambling,
'ana themselves in the poorhouse. The former not only
Provide for themselves, but also for the necessities of the
mate while the latter are simply pensioners on their
oiinty. Gian led that some are in the workhouse from
mstortune rather than from thriftlessness or vice, si ill I
'Him the number would be small. Anyhow, taxation and
l' t )| c se ntati° , i are no longer to be correlated. Vi bat is to
the practical outcome of this freak ? The pauper vote
, . v ®rpool would greatlv outnumber that of the free
utm', 6 'Pendent who earn their own subsistence outside,
i. 1 e , at of Parramatta would probably be one-half as
lai Sc, &c., &c.
Wrong ! 0, corpulent Tory ; altogether wrong!.
Men are the architects of their own fortunes
still, and they rise on the stepping-stones of
dead depositors to higher things. But, alas !
it isn’t the individuals who were addicted to
“guzzling, gobbling and gambling ” who are in
the asylums, nor the intelligent workmen, en-
dowed with industry, thrift and self-denial,
who are outside. Nobody makes or can make
a competence by pure industry and honesty—
by digging, or pick-and-shovel business, or
other honourable employments of the same sort;
and it is the broken-down handicraftsman and
digger and pick-and-shovel gentleman who crowd
up the benevolent institutions. Almost anybody
can make a competence by being a bloated
guzzler with a totalisator, and such men
rarely get into a benevolent institution
in their old age. The D.T.’s corres-
pondent, in fact, has got the situation in an
inverted sort of way, and his facts are all
standing on their heads. More paupers come
to their sad fate through steady industry and
thrift than through any other cause. Some of
them got crippled and blasted through being
industrious in mines, and many more were
wrecked through falling off high scaffoldings
while toiling on tall buildings. A large num-
ber were thrifty, earned very little, spent very
little, and saved very little, for 50 years, and
then somebody bolted with their savings in
their old age, and if they had drunk the money
instead they would have died in middle life,
and never reached any poor old age at all.
Almost all of them came to grief through some
sort or variety of hard work, but the individual
who got disabled while loafing, or reached a
patriarchal age and became a venerable pauper
through hard eating, is a myth. The assump-
tion of the average Tory wdio writes to the
Telegraph, that a man who is poor must be
base and lazy, and that a man who is rich must
be honourable and industrious, is an obvious lie,
else the average monarch would bo dead-
broke, and the asylums would be full of
monarchs, while the ’bus-driver who works
16 hours a day for £2 a week would be a mil-
lionaire, and live in a marble palace. There-
fore, when the paralysing question arises, “Is
the pauper to have a vote ?” The Bulletin
respectfully answers Yes. It probably took
him 40 years of incessant labour and unfailing
honesty to become a pauper, and it would be
mean and ungrateful for his country to go back
on him in his old age. And in taking such a view,
this paper acts upon solid Scriptural authority.
When the Lord pronounced a blessing on the
poor He heaved His anathema at the rich and
the conservative and the people who write to
the Daily Telegraph. Ho did that because
He knew how little Judicious fraud and loafing
were required to make a capitalist, and what
unspeakable airs the capitalist put on by reason
of his loafing.

Immoral Combinations.
It would never do to let a number of men combine as
they pleased to interfere with the just rights and natural
liberties of others. — Brisbane Observer.
That certainly would never do—or, rather,
ought not to do ; but it is done daily. Pre-
liminarily— wanted, definitions. What are
“just rights and natural liberties?” The
Observer would tell you “ the right of any
man to sell or buy labour on any terms agreed
upon ” —which means the right to accept the
least which the man on top offers, or to starve.
“ Just rights and natural liberties ’’ include the
right of every law-conforming citizen to live
by his labour on full feed and during healthy
hours, and the chance to develope himself
physically and mentally to the extremest
possibility of his stature and dimensions.
Now, let us see who they be who “ com-
bine as they please to interfere with the just
rights and natural liberties of others.” The
monopolists. How' are prices of commodities
regulated ? By supply and demand ? No.
By “rings” and “corners” and capitalistic
combinations? Yes. The Adelaide, Melbourne,
and Sydney millers have been notorious for
their skilful manipulation of the flour market.
Their operations have put thousands of pounds
into their own pockets —the pockets of a score
—at the expense of thousands of people, thou-
sands of struggling toilers to wdiom any sudden
misfortune meant ruin. Was this a legal com-
bination ? What town householder has not had
a severe experience of the greed of the coal-
rina's, that even out of the extreme misery and
destruction of the big maritime stri ke extracted
merriment and hard cash —aye, and boasted
of it and gloated over it? Who needs to be
reminded of the steamship “ pools that
now squeeze the public like an orange ?
What about carrying-company combinations ?
What gas-user hasn’t been defrauded by gas amal-
gamations ? How about tram companies ? 1 o
say nothing of banks, stock exchanges, land
rings, and a hundred other capitalistic mono-
polies that are cupping the community of its
life-blood? These combinations that “ interfere
with the just rights and natural liberties
of others ” are everywhere strewn around.
Yet, papers like the Brisbane Observer
chant paeans of praise to their “ laudable
enterprise,” and tell us that all this ’ pro-
putty,” acquired bv moral fraud and chicanery,
is “sacred,” that it has “rights” to be vene-
rated, and that to challenge its tenure is to
introduce chaos. It is debatable whether chaos
itself would not be more intrinsically just than
the present lattice-work of robberies. The
Observer itself is in “combination” with
other capitalistic papers, and can’t stand on its
own feet. The Brisbane newspaper proprietors
and printing houses are all in this a ery category
of men wdio “ combine as they please, I he
vials of wrath of the Observer “ and sich ” are
poured out only upon the workman. These
conditions cannot continue. They are daily
changing. And the first combinations to be
put down are those of the monopolists them-
selves the horse-leeches wdio combine to bleed
the masses wdiite, and yet shriek of injustice
w lien workers unite.

The Sydney Morning Herald wants to know
if “ the Labour-party would think it equally
right for another large party to sit in the Acw
South Wales Parliament, simply and avowedly
as representatives of the interests of Capital ? •
This question is superfluous. The Labour-paity
has been returned in consequence of a wide-
spread idea that previous Houses sat simply,
though not by any means “avowedly, m the in-
terests of Capital. The party’s blunt avowal that
it has been returned in the interests of Labour
while believing that when these interests aie
best served the whole community is benentc
will possibly have the effect of unmasking those
who have, heretofore, served Capital only and
claimed to serve all classes. The Herald
then insinuates that N.S. Wales is going to the
dogs, because a number of measures are being
brought forward, “intended to secure special
a ’vantage to a certain class at the expense of
( .her classes.” Had the Herald spoken truly,
or possessed the faculty of seeing clearly on the
question, it would have said that these
measures were calculated to remove certain dis-
advantages under which “ a certain class (the
wage earners) laboured, and to deprive ‘ other
classes” (the landlords, employers and usurers)
of certain privileges of which they have become
unjustly possessed, and which are now held in
defiance of humanity and popular outcry.
The Herald objects to the time of Parliament
being so largely occupied with democratic
reform. For this the Labour-party are not to
blame, but Parkes and the other Ministers,
who have for years promised these reforms,
while religiously keeping them in the back-
ground. The representatives of Labour have
simply opened the flood-gates that allow the
long dammed-back tide of necessary reform to
pour through. The Herald objects “to the
demoralising courting, by word or act, of the
Labour-party. ” That will be unnecessary when
the party has a majority in the House, then
the Herald may rest in peace.
The general traffic manager of the Queensland
Railway Department recently ukased that
“ officers, including stationmasters, superin-
tendents, inspectors and telegraph-operators,”
should sever their connection with the Queens-
land Railway Employees’ Association “ as at
present constituted.” When pressed to dis-
close the gravamen of the objection to the
Q.R.E.A., the official reply was that the experi-
ence of the Commissioners was “ that strict
discipline cannot be so long as
officers and men are permitted to join the same
labour organisation.” This is the bogy of the
marine-officers-and-seamen coalition resurrected
and transplanted to railway soil. It might as
well be urged that it undermines discipline for
officers and men to belong to the same lodge of
Masons, of Oddfellows, of Buffaloes, or even of
Good Templars. Yet we see this phase of
equality every day. Among the tars of the
British squadron there are many Good Templars,
for instance, and in lodge there are common
seamen who are Templar officers, empowered
to sit upon their own ship’s officers if the latter
transgress any lodge rules. But Jack obeys
Brassbuttons just as cheerfully and loyally
when outside lodge meetings. The fact is that
Railway Commissioners Mathieson, Johnston
and Gray are toadying to the fads of Lawyer-
Premier Griffith, the exclusive, and Sir Thos.
MTlwbaith, the haughty. Minister for Rail-
ways Unmack has admitted that the Commis-
sioners’ order was issued “ with the full know-
ledge and sanction of the Cabinet, after
discussion and consideration.”

The latest New South Wales loan is a dreary
commentary on the prosperity that is born of
Foreign-trade, and on the hideous financial
muddle left by M'Mxllan, that alleged heaven-
born person whom the Foreign-trade press used
to hold up as the bright and shining light of
finance. New South Wales, as we write, wants
to borrow 44 millions at one jump, and if the
money is not forthcoming the province will
thereupon be up a tree, for the late Treasurer
left £1,800,000 of urgent liabilities behind him,
and there are £1,500,000 of old debentures to
be retired. Therefore the loan is to be
floated at a minimum of 95, and an extra
half-year’s interest will be thrown in, making
the real minimum about 93. If the loan
aces off the country loses over £300,000,
and o-ets only about £1,000,000 cash in hand in
return, which looks like buying ready money
very dear ; and if the loan doesn’t go off, then
N S.W. files its schedule. It is the same old
story. When a Treasurer retires in a hurry
owinv to ill-health or urgent private business,
it is about 24 chances to one that he is clearing
out because he has pawned the national shirt,
and is at his last financial gasp ; and therefore
he wants to hand over the muddle to his suc-
cessor. And the successor floats a loan grace-
fully because he isn’t responsible for the
shortage ; and when his turn comes he also
quits owing to ill-health, or something, and his
successor finds another shortage, and floats
another loan. And every Treasurer, when he
comes in, is going to put things straight, and
keep them so for ever, and nearly every
Treasurer, when he goes out, goes out hurriedly
because of ill-health. They are an unhealthy
crowd.

monstrous —pass the harp, if you please.
In the decade 1879-89 there was a decrease
in the number of first-class passengers on British
and Irish railways from 39,000,000 to 30,000,000 ;
a fall in the number of second-class passengers
from 03,431,000 to 02,878,000 ; but an increase in
the number of third-class passengers of no less
than 222,000,000! The democracy does move,
after all.
A cake of gold, valued at over £4OOO, from
the New Pinafore claim, was exhibited in an
ironmonger’s window in Brisbane-street, Laun-
ceston, last week. On the strength of the sight a
man borrowed half-a-crown to “wet the crush-
ing.” Two months ago he held some hundreds ot
shares in the company, but forfeited them through
his inability to pay calls. Shares sold at 335. the
day he borrowed the coin.

Gibbon, by the way, means a monkey. Fitz

Dear Bulletin, —I notice in your weekly
by-references to the Irish Home Rule movement
that, whilst attributing the anti-Parnellite result
of the late by-elections to clerical influences, you
also attribute these influences to sinister motives.
Allow me to tell you that when you attribute
such motives to the great body of the Irish priest-
hood, you give your readers an exhibition of the
most glaring ignorance of the social status of, at
least, an important section of the Irish people.
It is, I know, unnecessary for me to inform you
that the root of all the discontentedness to be
found in Ireland is of an agrarian nature. While
the Irish farmers are constrained to support the
coffee-palaces and brothels of London and Paris,
for the delectation of their absentee landlords,
they and their families have frequently to eke out
an existence on potatoes [and Indian corn-meal —
a mode of livelihood that is naturally a bye-word
throughout the English-speaking world. Now,
as the great body of the Irish clergy come from
the middle-class farmers, would you kindly apply
to them the words of Virgil :—“ Non ignarus
mali, disco succurrere miseris.” From the time
they leave the cradle till they leave their father’s
home, they are unwilling spectators of the land-
lord lash under which they see their parents
wince. I take it for granted you admit they are
men _then don’t tell me that men educated in the
school of adversity, as they have been, can be so
dead to every human feeling as to pursue a course
that would be in direct antagonism to the welfare
of those who are related to them by every tie that
he world holds sacred. —Yours, etc., Blayney.
One Father Moore, of Melbourne, has
proved to the satisfaction of all good, confiding
Catholics that the Holy Coat at Treves is the real
maginnis. He tells the sweetest little yarn of
how a certain Empress Helena picked up the coat
(for a mere song, no doubt) on a pilgrimage to
Jerusalem, made for the special purpose of dis-
covering relics. Before, or after, annexing the
sacred garment, Helena found three crosses, and
was sore perplexed as to which was the right one.
So she routed around Jerusalem until she fell over
a sick woman, or, say a woman readied up for the
occasion by the Hebrew all-sorts gentleman who
had Helena in tow. Whereupon the Empress
prodded the sufferer with each cross in turn,
“ and when the third cross touched her she was
made well instantly.” “And so,” says good, old
Father Moore, “there is no doubt that that was
our Lord’s Cross.” Then, with a somewhat
sobered air of conviction, he adds —“It is not
probable that the coat which the Empress Helena
obtained as Christ’s coat was other than the true
coat.” Why, certainly not. Nothing is probable
except that Helena was an imperial idiot to go
and die in the ordinary way she did, when she
might have taken the cross back to Rome with
her, and have sat on it till the crack of Doom. As
for the few dealers who kept such a safe cure in
stock without administering a thump to the local
invalids—well, their disgraceful conduct seems to
indicate that they were embarked in the under-
takering line, and regarded immortality as
inimical to business.
The Australasian, 'in an article on the
Legal Profession Amalgamation Bill :
The distinction between the barrister and the solicitor,
between the public advocate and the man who prepares
the case and sees the witnesses and does all the routine
work necessary for a trial in the law courts, has existed
for centuries, and may even be said to go back to the days
of the Roman Forum. An arrangement which is thus
founded upon experience and has endured for many years
has surely something to recommend it.
A nice argument, this. Out upon the Roman
Forum ! Dirt and snakes are both older than the
Roman Forum, but they are just as objectionable
as if they were invented yesterday. The same
argument applies to lepers, and smashed hats, and
ulceration of the intestines, and if a thing must,
merely because it is old, have “ something to
recommend it,” then ulceration of the intestines
is obviously a noble institution. Therefore let us
whoop for ulceration of the intestines. And while
we are about it, let us raise three cheers for
murder, and sudden death, and premeditated
homicide, and ash-heaps —for old bones, bad
drainage, evil smells, the pig under the bed, the
a-oat in the rose-garden, stale fowls, ignorance,
dandruff, lies, paganism, nakedness, the slaughter
and eating of aged parents, alligators, pestilence,
worms old fish, slime, blasphemy, and decayed
eels for they are, all older than the Roman Forum,
and things that have endured so many years have
surely somethin?/ to recommend them. And the
only tiling, according to the Australasian s own
argument] that has nothing at all to recommend it
is the Australasian itself, for it isn't as old as
the Roman Forum or any other Forum—not by a
very large quantity.

816 Macquarie-slreet North, Sydney, Sept. 16,1891.
My Dear Moorabinda, —
Strictly spring weather for the first day of the Spring
Meeting of the A.J.C., at Randwick, on 12th inst., with
ransacked wardrobes as a consequence, of course. The
two vice-regalities—Lady J. and “Her Excellency” of
South Australia—were very nicely turned out. Lady
Jersey’s dress was of salmon-pink crepe cloth made with
a demi-train and trimmed with greenish silver floral gimp
applique, basque of fine cream-lace finishing the round
bodice, which was made frontally in folds from right to
left. Bonnet in gold and pearls trimmed fore and aft
with bunches of red currants and leaves, black tulle fall
and 6cru lace parasol. Lady Kintore (accompanied by
the gude mon, ye ken, in a necktie too fight for his terra-
cotta cheek) wore a pale-grey lustre, embroidered round
the feet with grey chenille, and pansies and leaves in
natural colours, single pansies dotting all over the front
of the skirt, a border of pansies and leaves trimming the
bodice, which had a front of mauve satin smocked at the
top. A narrow sash in mauve and green satin ribbon fell
at the back, and the etceteras were a small bonnet in gold
wire trimmed with velvet pansies and leaves, and tied
with short green velvet strings, a black parasol with a
white ivory cockatoo on the handle, a miraculous match
in grey gloves, and don’t let me forget the rough-edged,
unmounted single eye-glass Lady K. applied to her right
optic at moments of excitement. It was a dreadfully
crude effort—something like the “ eye-glass ” the broken-
booted masher evolves (by grating on the kerbstone) out
of the wreck of a tumbler.
The other women promenaded the cropped green lawn
in the frills and flowers which are so pretty after the
sombre winter things. White, grey and fawn were the
general colours, the number of white gamps on the grass
poetically suggesting plum-blossoms blown by the breezes.
The prettiest girl I saw on the course wore cream muslin
with a small eau-de-nil spot, eau-de-nil ribbons about it,
and a straw hat covered with white ostrich-feathers.
Another pretty girl (not quite so pretty as her sisters used
to be) had a bad fit in pearl grey and a big hat trimmed
with white crepe and yellow roses. Mrs. Rupert Caring"
ton (now looking quite matronly) had on a black dress,
the seams of the loose jacket braided with Hussar braid,
vest of mauve satin, lavender gloves, and a small bonnet
trimmed with violets and buttercups. By-the-way, how
is brother Rupert No. 1 going to wear his gold watch-
chain ? It is now laced across on a level with his armpits,
and if it keeps on rising will probably end its career as a
necklace. Black, powdered with pansies, and a ridiculou 3
“ little” bonnet en suite was smartly worn by a gay and
festive society matron with an Edith Bland profile •
rich grey satin and brocaded gown with Louis coat and
demi-train edged with grey ostrich feathers was a very
handsome shell for a rather unprepossessing “ nut” ; and
a lady in green, with a Jersey nose, had unwisely crowned
herself with pale pink roses. The victorious Stromboli
was paraded on the lawn with the Derby ribbon round his
neck, and the spurred blood on his ribs, and really one
deduced quite a solemn moral from the sight. v
To sum up Spring fashions as revealed at the races.
It is evident that novelties are scarce, and that old styles
will keep in till Summer. All the best women were demi-
trained, a taking mode for the lawn, supplemented by
the “kerridge,” but a draggle-tailed exhibition for the
block, followed by the twopenny tram, and don’t you
forget it. One or two wore high paniers and coat-tails,
but without an “ improver ’’—this style looks wet-hen-ish
caught sideways. Skirts are mostly plain gored and,
judging by the revealed play of limb, usually worn
without petticoats too; basques were frequent, and round
bodices loomed around, while anything seemed to be
“as worn” in the hat fine, from a bow and a bunch of
flowers to a pointed thing the size of an umbrella. The
whole secret lies in the way you put them on.
Apropos of the “feasts” at which “ladies” fly round
with cups and plates, it is said that some of these philan-
thropists have petty larceny in their eye all the time, and
seize a bun here and a banana there —stowing the spoil in
a pillow-case pinned under their skirts, I suppose. One
well-known “helper” is a terror at putting her dessert
up her sleeve, so the other evening, as she went skimming
after bonbons-“for the men, my dear!’’—somebody
solemnly remarked : “ Mind you don’t take them home
with you, Mrs. Blank.”
The committee of the N.S.VV. Art Society held their
annual conversazione, at their Pitt-street location, on
“ IX Sept.,” as the programme (designed with a wondrous
tangle of naked cherubs and jackasses by Madame Roth)
had it, and a surging throng crept in, of course. The
committee, in double bowing-line, received the visitors
as they tripped over the mat, and lightning sketches by
most of the artists present, and songs by Miss Josephine
O’Reilly, filled in the time very soothingly—though it
must have hurt one artist’s feelings, as he made his
“lightning” attempt in a bored way, to hear a shrill
female voice remarking, “ Oh ! I know what it’s going to
be ! I remember it! ” And yet they say all women have
tact.
Those who went to gaze on Art by night got the dazzle
of gas in the glass of the pictures, and the colours hope-
lessly bewitched all along the fine, so they gave it up and
socialised. Lady Innes, in nectarine-coloured silk con-
trasted with apricot guipure and embroidered chiffon, did
the walls assiduously, and Sir George Innes was there
smiling at his purchases. Lady X, extremely see-more
in white and blue streamers, rested a fat, pink arm up to
the elbow in the acuted angle presented for her support
by an artistic K.C.M.G. and did the gallery frame by
frame; the chiding judge who “won’t have it” and the
chidden counsel were there, obviously not “playing
chaneys,” though it is a physical impossibility that one
should loo> *<<fcr the other’s head with a cold, unseeing
glare. Madame Roth, in sage-green and high-gilt wire
collar, and a string of savage’s beads round her throat and
a cluster of daffodils at the belt, looked like the frontis
piece to an Art Journal. Mrs. J. R. Ashton (wife of the
President) wore black and white ; somebody with pretty
fair hair was mermaidish in seagreen fisher’s net; and the
wearer of a grey frock with chiffon frills and gloves en
suite tried hard to look magaziney, even to the extent of
sitting on one foot on the deep-backed couches. That’s
the worst of artists’ celebrations. All the women have
such a predisposition to leave their corsets off and ruffle
their maiden hair, that an ordinary creature with just her
Sunday frock on has a vague idea that she is a jarring
note.
With the men fringing round the Gaiety shrine, ‘twas
small wonder that it seemed all skirts at St. Vincent’s
ball, held in the Town Hall the same night, and at which
six hundred turned up. There was a little rush of men
after the theatres, and the naval contingent (all insatiable
deadheads, who invariably argue that because their men
take their toothaches to be cured at the hospital they
ought to pose as free-pass pillars of that worthy institu-
tion) did their duty even to barn-dance lengths, so, per-
haps, those bevies of seated damsels were only resting'
after all. Vice-regal patronage of the most advanced
type, of course ; Lady Jersey, smiling and amiable, in
ivory silk draped with cream lace at the feet, caught
with tarantula bows of green velvet; green velvet hows
on each shoulder, and diamond ornaments. Lady Kin-
tore in faded puce, brocaded with ox-eye daisies and
trimmed on the bodice with white chiffon, diamond orna-
ments. Lady Scott wore a pretty girly-girly gown in
pale-blue satin, the skirt shrouded in pale-blue tulle, with
festoons of pink roses at the feet, the coat-tail train of
blue satin, festoons of pink unmounted roses on the
corsage. Mrs. W. P. Manning (Mayoress) opened the ball
with the Governor, and wore a handsome black lace and
jet gown, diamond ornaments. The rest was dust and
ashes, generally writing, because a handful of nice gowns
(I like a saffron brocade with beetles’-wing trimming, and
another fellow, with buttercup epaulets and simile Vutur
cups studding the gauze front suggested poetry) make me
forget a fat black-haired matron classically gotten up in
a red wrapper hauled in at the 4C-inch waist by a white
girdle ; or another Katisha in pea-green satin and a baby
sash tied high ; or a much-painted female in a mulberry
chiton and no corset, who had forgotten, or never meant,
to shake the powder out of her ears. The band was
rather erratic in time, and the floor rather fast, but the
supper was too well done tor a charity spec, as usual, and
among the people one wanted to smile at were Dr. de
Lambert, Mr. “Jack” Nathan, Mr. Wagner (from Mel-
bourne), M. J. Barlow, Mr. and Madame Kellermann —
and, of course, Dan O’Connor.
The Gaiety Company’s first fusillade of dangerous
puns at the Theatre Royal on Monday was certainly
the event of the evening—the place was powdered
with Governors’ aide-de-camps and things like that—
Wallington silhouetting his Wellingtonian profile in a
prompt box filled with a buck party, the boyish Earl of
Ancrum, in his element between two society girls ; and
society faces, led to the slaughter at seven-and-sixpence
per head, rose dimly from every velvet chair or perched
uncomplainingly on any ledge that offered. Neverthe-
less—perhaps because there were many men—the house
didn’t scintillate, except where the electric-light shone
on the gold-wash, don’t you know. The Hill party had
Mrs. Rupert Carington in the midst thereof in a
salmon-pink gown and opera-wrap edged with 6cru
feathers ; Miss Nellie Hill wore a black lace and silk gown
with electric-green butterfly in the hair. Mrs. S. A. Moss,
in the 0.P.-box, wore black velvet; and Mrs. (Dr.)
Schwartzbach with her, in dark red. Our Frisky in the
front row, as usual, wore cream, and the most elegant
woman there had a dim seat in the back row, but which
was moighty convanient for foregathering with nice
young men over the rail during the short waits.
In “Ruy Bias,” pretty Florence Levey and dainty
Sylvia Grey had nearly all the dressing, though Nellie
Farren (somebody behind me, of course not a woman,
enthused that she hadn’t altered an eyelash in the last 20
years) wore a taking black and silver rig on her first
rapturously-received entrance ; and a violet velvet and
satin cap and gown with a glitter of diamonds, was
festive. In the first act, Levey, who dances like a daffo-
dil, wore a rather “suburban” effect in pink with
caterer’s-decoration-like garlands of white flowers, but
Sylvia Grey (an hilarious medico in the circle—which his
name is Mac., and he is getting bald fast, poor man—wildly
shrieked—" Oh, you beauty 1” as she finished her first
pas seul) wore a lovely frock in sulphur-green silk with
sprays of wistaria on the bodice, the Turkish effect under-
neath in purple silk edged with single flowers, and a waist,
scarf of white spangled in silver giving an airy effect as
she kicked. And, by the way, why don’t lady Thespiennes
who turn the scale at 16-stun exercise as danseuses in
their off time ? If it enlarges the calves, it certainly
spiderises the party from thence up’ards.
The “ Arts and Science ” Exhibition in connection with
the Sydney Y.M.C.A. was opened to slow music on Bth
inst., when a good crowd thronged in. The show was not
at all fine-drawn, and what surprised me about it was the
manner in which water was shown up in its bonniest
fight. In the model-department, there was some>water
in a glass bulb with the air extracted, and which made an
awful racket (like somebody spilling tacks) when shaken
up and down, and the principal microscopic draw was a
drop of water, in which horned blue monsters with ten
tails went swarming about. Now, this didn’t strike me
as being a good way to lure the members on to the filter.
An interesting exhibit was a five black-and-red snake —
the first time, in all probability, that a snake was ever
seen at the Y.M.C.A.—and a fire-screen, designed in
raised waratahs and foliage, was beautifully worked*
Is it true that an Australian M.P. is going to remove
his butterfly spouse some miles back, out of touch with
her crowd of Petrarchs? If he does, he should photo-
graph every Lovelace on his retina, or else he may be
tricked as was another gentleman who carted his flirty
pansy-blossom a “ hundred miles from the railway line,’’
where the giddy moths soon forgot her, except one who
roamed along one day in the guise of a jewellery-
drummer. He had only disguised his fatal beauty under
a red moustache, but hubby entertained him because “ he
could see he was a gentleman ” —bought a watch from
him, but neglected to set one on him, and—but what is
the use of sketching the result ?
Sydney citizens—instigated by Rev. Mr. Shearston, of
Trafalgar House, ably seconded by Mayor Manning-
entertained the petty officers and crews of the Britisli
fleet at high tea and a concert in the Town Hall, on 10th
inst. Provision was made for 700, but only GOO turned
up at the nicely laid and decorated tables set in the base-
ment. Dozens of helpful damsels spilt gravy and vege-
tables all over poor Jack in a winning amateur way, and
even went to the length of pinning nosegays into his flap
collar. The cross table at the top (under the care of Mrs.
Berry Drinan) had the Mayor for centre-piece with Mr.
Daniels, Mayor Stokes, Rev. Shearston and others
scattered around, and although the quenchers were only
tea and coffee the break-up was very lively—marines
waltzing together, and some of the tars barn-dancing
when the cigars began to circulate.
At the concert in the upper hall a large party had seats
on the platform. Sir “ Precedent” was there, gold-bound
as usual, taking copious notes with a silver pencil. Lady
Scott (who doesn’t like Hobart-or can it be the Hobart
people?—well enough to put herself to the trouble of
going there this time) looked unutterably bored in a mul-
berry gown toned with black guipure, jet bonnet, and
half fall. Mrs. Manning (Mayoress), in heliotrope and
bonnet en suite, held the beautiful bouquet of English
primroses, violets, ferns, and snowdrops presented by the
sailors ; and two well-known Sydney ladies in the pat-
ronising party wore evening dress, but wisely kept their
cloaks on when they saw how the cat jumped. The
artistes contributing comprised Colhourne-Baber (who
secured an enthusiastic encore and a bouquet for her
“ Kerry Dance ”), some of the Sydney Liedertafel filters*
Mr. Schoou (encored for his cornet solo), and M. Wiegand,
and, of course, there were speeches to the gallant tars,
who presented rather a Bacchanalian appearance with
wreaths of flowers around their necks-some with three
and four white lilies in their jumpers. Which reminds
me that some of the sailors took the floral display for an
indigenous effort, and one man held up a pink camelia
with the query—“ Do these yere grow in the fields,
miss ?”
Governor Jersey isn’t reputedly a plunger, hut it cer-
tainly looked rather suggestive to sec Ills Ex. foot-
padding it home on Derby-day through the Centennial
Park. It gave him a dead-broke aspect as if the hollow
joy of “picking them” had induced him to render up
even his cab-fare, and his bools w ere dusty and his hat
ssemed several sizes too much, and if he hadn’t been a
banker-governor anybody would have taken him to he
the ordinary “knowing” race-frequenter suffering a bad
attack of disillusion. Which reminds me that the
Sydney high-world coterie have of late taken to
“Shanks’ pony” with great enthusiasm—possibly to
reduce undue avoirdupois with an eye to the subjugation
of the Gaiety girls, who may cherish an objection to
tickling double chins.
A certain Australian Legislative Council has at least one
member at the present time who is unable to conform to
the modern prejudice that requires a man to he able to
write his own name, or that of his rich employer. A
candidate for a coming L T pper House election is not going
to have this slur cast upon him though, and on com-
mencing his campaign he placed himself under the tuition
of an able instructor in caligraphy. Success appears to
have crowned his efforts, for lie can, with only three
weeks’ practice, laboriously scrawl his cognomen on a
sheet of paper after the manner of Brough in “ Dandy
Dick.” The tutor has hopes that by the time the election
comes off, his pupil will be able to write with his tongue
in his mouth, and without his front hair in the ink bottle-
One skeleton in the cupboard is generally as much as
most men canstand, but the man who bought Dr. Beaney’s
celebrated skeleton evidently thinks that one more or
less in the house doesn’t matter.
A penchant for other men’s wives appear to run in
families. A very near relative of a youth who not long
before his death figured as co-respondent in a Riverine
divorce-suit, heard in Sydney, lately visited Melbourne,
and returned to England by a recent mail, not alone. The
children and the injured husband were carefully left
behind.
The original owner of a station (at which an Australian
Governor was lately entertained) and grandfather of a
lady who, the other day, made what is generally regarded
as rather a brilliant match, lost all his money and friends,
and for some years before his death was an ostler at a
Murray River shanty. The present owner of the station
commenced fife as an ostler in Purcell’s Horse
Bazaar. This lively stable has been a mascotte, and more
than one millionaire commenced fife here as a valet des
chevaux—beginning with Tommy Monahan, of St. Hilda,
and including—a St. Hilda squatter who owns magnificent;
N.S. VV. property; the late richly-gilded Mickey Dawson
(before he went into private practice), father-in-law
of Fitz Gibbon and a certain millionaire ex-coach,
whip, not unknown in connection with Mount Morgan.
Mr. Charles A. Harris, lately with Dr. Slate, has
begun the practice of his profession as a dentist at
St. James’ Chambers, top of King-street, Sidney, with
a boy in buttons, a suite of artistic rooms, afternoon
tea for lady sufferers, and a charming lady-assistant who
holds the patient’s hand and sees that the reviving spray
doesn’t take the crimp out of her front hair. Mr. Harris
is an enthusiast, and has several new ideas. Nervous
people wifi all like the artificial teeth that can be screwed
into stumps in the gums instead of having those hard and
fast nuisances pulled out.
Fanny Enson’s benefit at the Criterion on 9th inst. drew
the usual crowd of women solemnly chewing chocolate
with their Sunday frocks on, there being the same per-
centage of men present as there are stones to a grocer’s
pound of plums. The “Man-woman” was there, with
her arms folded, and one lady, evidently not a matinee-
haunter, came in a white evening dress with kid gloves,
but quenched her glory under an ulster as she grasped
the tailor-built situation. On the stage, Mrs. Brough, in
the Screen scene from “School for Scandal,” wore a cen-
tury dress in ivory satin and yellow ; but Cates, as the
naughty man, was altogether too ethereal for a black-
satin suit—beside the paniers of Lady T. he looked like
the shadow of a stick of licorice.
Miss Frances Cogdon (with a dot of £60,000) was united
in the bonds of matrimony to Mr. Alec. MTntyre, in
Melbourne, the other day. Alec is fair, fat and 50, and
might be mistaken at a cursory inspection for Duncan
Gillies. His claims for distinction rest principally on the
fact that he was an adorer of the famous Emelie Melville
company in their palmiest days. The lady is the only
daughter of the late Police-magistrate Cogdon, the
handsomest man in Ballarat, in the early sixties (they do
say he was brought out of his room at Craig’s with his
beard in curl-papers) ; the cash, however, comes from her
grandfather, Bob Threkeld, an old Melbourne identity,
whom all old colonists will remember at Kirk’s livery-
stables, and afterwards at the Saracen’s Head. Alec has
not been treated with reckless generosity in the matter of
settlements.
A very fashionable marriage parade was nipped
in the bud recently by the death of the
bride’s grand - aunt or somebody. This was most
aggravating, after the bridegroom had arranged to
pay £5O (of the bride’s money) to ring the Cathedral
chimes all day ; another £5O out of the same purse, for
retaining the services of his Lordship the Bishop—and a
like sum for the assistant supernumeraries. The wedding,
however, came off directly the funeral was over ; so that
the funeral baked meats served tor the wedding-breakfast,
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
The annual ball given by the East Zetland Masonic
Lodge at West’s rooms on 3rdi nstant, added another one
to the list of successful Sydney Masonic balls held this
year, commencing 8.30 and ending next day. Mr. J.
Harris represented the Grand Lodge in wonderful regalia,
and Mrs. Nicholls wore the handsomest dress—yellow silk
and seed pearls. The supper was very well done.
Mrs. W. P. Manning’s dance is to he held in Sydney Town
Hall to-night (Thursday). It is expected to’degenerate
into a hall.
Manly Wild Flower Show takes place on 23rd instant,
and the usual floral ferment is now going on at Our
Village. Discreet matrons sail forth in spring carts at
early morn to catch the lurking waratah in his lair.
Gov. and Lady Jersey entertain the members of the
Synod at a garden party at Government House on 25th
instant. Interesting that the Synod and the A.J.C. meet
at the same time and season !
Lord and Lady Jersey, with the Admiral and Lady
Scott, shortly pay a visit of inspection to “Trafalgar
House,” the sailors' home, under the direction of Mission-
ary Shearston.

Not having been seen in Sydney for
about four years, “ lolanthe ’’.proved a trump card
that might have been turned-up to advantage at
an earlier period. Crowded houses followed it
from start to finish, and on Saturday, the closing
night, when the Comic Opera Co.' received an
enthusiastic send-off, much money was turned
away.
* * *
Of “ Ruy Bias” it may be said in the
words of a German critic : “ This may funny be
in the land of roast-beef and plum-pudding, but
vulgarly stupid and inartistic seems it here.” It
is simply highly-gilt bosh, with even less con-
tinuity of purpose and ingenuity of situation than
“Evangeline,” chock-full of stage-tricks that are
stale, and puns that are passe, albeit brightly
carried off by folks whose gifts might have been
more fitly devoted to higher branches of the art to
amuse. Even when viewed as an avenue for men
to discover how women look when partially divested
of the delusiyeaids to contour supplied bymilliners
burlesque fails in what might be assumed to be its
purpose, for the improvements provided by pads
and puffs, tucks and pigments, are calculated to
mislead the most critical eye that ever blinked
through an opera-glass. “ The proper study of
mankind is woman 1” Yes, but when you place
50 or 100 women before an audience of men there
is no opportunity for critical study or analysis.
Two shapely limbs are more seductive than a hun-
dred so-so ones, just as a nicely-cooked gigot
right under your nose is more appetising than
fifty legs of mutton hanging up in the butcher’s.
Woman, collectively and in the raw, is always
inane, usually tedious.
* * *
All the Gaiety folks, especially Farren
and Leslie, were warmly welcomed back. We
don’t see much alteration in any of them, either
for good or bad, except, perhaps, in Storey, whose
new eccentric dance seems more suggestive of com-
plete dislocation than anything he did here during
the prior visit. Of the new people, Grace Pedley
is no great vocalist, but sings pleasingly. She got
warmly encored by an easily-pleased house on
opening for “The Song that reached my Heart,’’
though, with the surroundings, “The Bloke that
sneaked my Tart ” would have been more appro-
priate. Florence Levey is a very graceful dancer,
who achieved at least equal rank with Sylvia Grey
ere the conclusion of her first caper. Nelly
Farren, suffering badly, so ’tis said, from influenza,
was just as raucous and as popular as ever, and
Leslie still travels on the bald-head racket. Danby
got a big encore for “ After Me,” sung in a back-
slum, long-beer voice, but refused to sing “Bally-
hooly,” saying that if they (the audience) had not
forgotten it he had. One of the funniest things
in the show is the Leslie-Farren burlesque, on the
minstrel manner, in “ My mother told me so ” and
the alleged clog-dance that follows.
* # *
It would be just as well to have the various
gags localised, for the Sydney “ push ” don’t hold
much stock in the Eastern Market and Ballarat.
By the way, the scenery, especially of the first and
third acts (Gordon’s), is very beautiful.
* *- * *
There are some smart quips in “The
Ambassador.” The definition of a man of the
world as “One who knows better and does worse,
and so cosmopolitan that he embraces more women
than his wife,” for instance ; or this, “Moralists
say we’re never so happy as when we are good,
but the truth is that we’re never so good as when
we’re happy.” Despite this happiness of phrase,
however, it was unfortunately withdrawn in
favour of “The Middleman” on Saturday night.
Unfortunately, because there is such a lamentable
difference between the last and the first perform-
ance at Sydney Garrick of that peculiar play. Of
the original east, only five remain in the bill and
all as originally placed. They are excellent as ever;
For the others, let silence be the tombstone over
efforts abortive and uninteresting—nay, wearying.
Three nights of “ Tatlow ware ” were followed by
two of “The Profligate,” and one each “A Scrap
of Paper” and “The Idler.” “The Village
Priest ” goes up on Saturday for six nights, and
the Darrells open on the following Satm-day in, it
is said, “The Sundowner,” while Cartwright and
Co. fly to Brisbane, where they play a twelve-
night season, returning to Melbourne for a fort-
night ere sailing for England.
** * *
Sydney Her Majesty’s was visited by the
officers of the auxiliary squadron on Saturday
night last, but their presence in the dress-circle
failed to induce that influx of the fair sex that
might have been anticipated, and so they sat and
glowered at each other in solitary silence dismally
thinking what heaps of loveliness they were wast-
ing on a desert bare. George Rignold celebrated
his fourth year of management by giving away
souvenirs, varying in value from a shilling to fifteen
guineas, on Monday last, but, dripping wet from
his dive into the many tons of water that compose
the Regent’s Canal, was obliged to depute Harry
Jewett to speak a piece for him to the clamorous
audience. Wednesday night closed the season,
and the Company skipped on the following day to
Melbourne. Laura Yilliers opens on Saturday m
“ A Celebrated Case.”

The benefit given to Fanny Enson, prior
to her departure to England, was scarcely so well
attended as the occasion deserved. Bessie Kig-
nold opened by singing “Lights o London
Town” from the orchestra; then Win. Rignold
successfully burlesqued the manner of the very
heavy tragedian in “T. he Lights, aided by Her
Majesty’s Co. Old Cathcart, as bir Peter, ovei-
lapped his associates; Mrs. Brough, Messrs.
Cates. Greenaway and Welch, in the screen-scene
from “ The School for Scandaland the Criterion
Co aided by Myra Kemble and Emma Gwynne,
played a slab of a not very effective act from
“Impulse.” The Sasses played their “Umbrella
comedietta; Harry Jewett finely recited Shamus
O’Brien,” and Louise Davenport trotted out the
trashy, toadying “Kissing Cup’s Race,”
of tomrnyrot in which there is at least one sir
in each line. Ryley sang “ For Love of Thee,
and Elton did “ The Dashing Mihtaire.” Results
at treasury, respectable.
** * *
Every one should applaud organist
Wiegand for having the courage of his opinions in
refusing to play nothing but the “ ugly ” music of
the so-called advanced school of the day. The
Bullei’lN has no patience with the twaddle talked
by some people on this subject. Wild, weird,
long-haired cranks rush up and down the piano,
producing a complete kaleidescope of hideous
chords and call it the ‘‘advanced
Violinists struggle wildly with helpless fiddles
and tear out wails that might well be taken for
the cries of a porker in distress, and this forsooth
they tell us is the new school ! Melody, pure and
simple, is regarded with ineffable scorn and yet
Bach, Beethoven, Mozart, Handel, Haydn,
Mendelssohn and Weber wrote it!
