According to War Cry advertisements
admission will be charged to General Booth’s
coming performances, in Sydney Centenary Hall,
at the following rates i
Prices of Admission: Season tickets, admitting to a*
meetings, ss. each ; reserved scats for single meeting, 2s
each; unreserved seats. Is each; officers’season tickets,
Is each. Silver coin collections at the doors at Monday
and Sunday meetings. Everyone expected to give.
According to this ad. visitors will not only be
charged for admission, but “expected to give a
silver coin ”on coming out! What are the Social
Purists going to do who object to all charges made
for Sunday amusements as the best way of putting
an end to competition with the ecclesiastical show
business?
** * *
The Dampiers finished up their season at
Adelaide with that old, invariable yell of agony,
“East Lynne.” It came off with all its ac-
customed vigour ; but the old play badly wants to
be re-written. If Levison’s bell-topper were a
shade less shiny, and if Lady Isabel would swear
in her exile instead of weeping with such a damp,
oppressive weep, and if she would return to find
her heir putting a head on another boy, instead of
snuffling in the last stage of Sunday-school con-
sumption, and if Archibald Carlyle could be run
over by a bullock-dray before the play begins, and
somebody could come on in the first act and
mention casually that he is dead, things would be
much better. Anvhow, the company escaped
alive, and will reproduce Mrs. Wood’s dreary
shriek at Broken Hill, unless a justly-outraged
Providence intervenes, and the scenery is struck
by lightning, and the takings are blasted by the
artillery of heaven. Tanner has finished his fast.
He was a slow exhibition while he lasted, and the
sight of a thin and hollow human being sitting in
a chair, and thinking mournfully of provisions,
grows wearisome in course of time. Johnel
.Sullivan has also gone to Adelaide. His reception
in that mild, quiet, grandmotherly city, and how
he puts a tail on the audience, and the number of
waiters he has killed, and how he goes home and
gambols with his mighty feet on the ruins of the
supper, and prances amid the mustard and grease,
and dashes the furniture to Sheol and back again,
when business is bad, may possibly be further
alluded to on some future occasion. With the ear
of faith we can hear the hollow crash of his
arrival, and the groans of the ticket-taker who
committed suicide by asking Johnel for his ticket,
and we can just make out afar off the noise of
John wrecking his cab with his fists because the
driver doesn’t go fast enough. Williamson’s
Comic Opera Co. will open shortly in the “Gon-
doliers,” and Stacey starts at Garner’s Rooms
with “Hans the Boatman,” as soon as billiardist
Roberts vacates. Monty Brown, manager for
this latter show, loolced with a cold, injured
expression upon The Bulletin’s local representa-
tive, and declined to let him in. This was mis-
placed humility upon his part. The Bulletin
man is not at all haughty, and if he had gone in
he would have associated with the manager on
equal terms, and wouldn’t have looked down on
him in the least. The pressman who would hurt
the feelings of a humble but well-meaning
theatrical individual takes a base advantage_ of
his superiority, and is unfit to be an Australian
citizen.
* * *
Melbourne Princess’ was ominously un-
crowded on Saturday night, when “The Late
Lamented,”—a title bearing no reference to the
lost Gaiety Company—put in a first appearance.
Adapted from “Feu Toupinal,” by Mr. Fred.
Horner, this farcical comedy is a maze of mis-
understandings, and a joke that seems too in-
geniously complex for a weak-minded audience.
Undoubtedly, it is a clever piece of work. Horner
lias put in his thumb and pulled out all the best
French plums from the original pie to flavour his
English version.. But much of the fun, being of
the subtle kind that requires quick appreciation
and rapid consumption, was lost in the rough and
insufficiently rehearsed performances of Saturday.
“The Late Lamented” was a mercantile person
named Nicholson, with mutton-chop whiskers,
who divided his domestic life into equal parts and
left a couple of widows to revere the same
memory, unbeknown to one another—a tame, but
determined widow in London, and a wild, frisky
little relict on the island of Cyprus. The
bereaved females marry again, as women will
under slight provocation, and when the curtain
rises they are living on two different flats in West
End mansions, with the two different flats who
(also unbeknown to one another) are filling poor
dear Nicholson’s two pairs of shoes. In each
instance the course of true second love runs
smooth although the husbands offer some
slight objections, on principle, to the constant
laying of floral tributes on Nicholson’s sarco-
phagus, more especially as they themselves are
always pressed into the solemn pilgrimage to the
bone-yard, and compelled to carry the basket. The
widows never happen to meet on these occasions,
nor to notice that somebody else has been strewing
flowers over Nicholson’s mouldering dust. It is
possible that boys promptly come and steal the
blossoms immediately after each visit. Portraits
of Nicholson, in his habit as he lived at “ home ”
and in Cyprus, hang in the best room of either
flat, and that commonplace impostor is decaying
in an odour of sanctity, so to speak, until an old
military mash of the larky Mrs. N. comes visiting
the gentleman who took over the other Mrs. N.
The misleading disclosures made by this major
from Cyprus, assisted by elaborate misconstruc-
tions elsewhere and the general idiocy of a man-
servant produce two acts of tangled agony ter-
minating in the usual explanation. The ghost
of Nicholson gets into hot water as the curtain
falls. An uneasy and, in many cases, ill-fitted
cast made but a poor start with “ The Late
Lamented,” as already mentioned. Two or three
of them may be expected to put more dash and
breadth into their performances forthwith, but it
is unlikely that a harmonious tout ensemble can
ever obtain under present circumstances. Gour-
lay plays the principal husband in a comical yet
altogether uncanny style, too much like that of a
genteel clown out of pantomime. The method is
irreconcileable with anybody else’s. His red wig,
stage voice, facial make-up, and tragic comedy
incline to the supernatural, and are distinctly out
of touch with modern civilisation. Brough would
put a far happier construction on the same part.
Maggie Moore, as the leading wife, is fast tpning
herself down to light comedy requirements,
and manages to make a partial success, but
Maggie Knight and Hans Phillips infuse very
little life into the minor married couple.
The monotony of Royce’s gutturals doesnt
dim his popularity, as the drivelling old retainer,
whilst Snazelle seems likely to hit the mark after
a little more practice in the major character. He
started brightly enough on Saturday (in fact, the
whole first act was well, if slowly, played), but
missed his chances as time went on. A conviction
that his trousers were braced up six inches above
the normal level seemed to settle upon Snazzy s
soul and haunt his hilarity, until at last his talent
became small and insignificant as compared with
his boots. Nobody appeared to the manper of
the comedy born, with the one exception of Edwin
Kelly, who realised the fleeting character of
Nicholson’s lawyer with a tender care and delicate
humour that naturally had no charms for the
gallery. The quiet excellence of Kelly s acting,
a perfect concealment of art by art, was a treat
which very few seemed to appreciate. “The Late
Lamented” will scarcely last until “ The Merry
Duchess ” is ready to tune up. Yet there is real
grit in this show—mostly under the surface.
“Jane” is making very good wages at Melbourne
Bijou, and the place suits her, Jane’s place is as
unconventional as herself. She is parlour-maid to
a young bachelor in “rooms” where a man-
servant is kept. The bachelor, being a gay, reck-
less dog, doesn’t come home oftener than once a
fortnight, presumably, so one can t marvel that
William and Jenny should fill up the spare mean-
time by falling in love. William, we imagine,
can stand on the mat outside Jane’s door and sing
of his affections all through the livelong night,
without “master,” away on the razzle-dazzle,
being any wiser. A couple so situated would
naturally drift into matrimony. The reputation
of the “ rooms ” demands that fatal step. Pattie
Browne might be a less melo-dramatic adopted
parent of the rag-baby, but her general briskness
atones for much that is open to criticism, and the
damsel comes off triumphant in all respects.
Brough’s conception of the sat-upon spouse grows
more airy, Anson is the most gloriously insane
uncle that ever accepted a sham pledge of a flec-
tion, and the others help to make “Jane roll
along like an earthquake. No change for another
week or two. ,
*# * *
The absence of prima-donna Guidotti,
through sickness, has made itself painfully felt m
“ Les Huguenots ” at Melbourne Alexandra.
Madame Boema, the substitute in an all-important
character, seems to fish for her most difficult
notes, and they are not particularly fine when
landed. Nor does Madame fill the billet satis-
factorily in any other respect. The mischance
which has so marred this production of Meyer-
beer’s masterpiece is the more regrettable, because
of the evident intention to have ma_de_ it a first-
class entertainment. The fair Matioli is a nice
young queen, and Vilalta generally gets there
with his tenor music in the face of difficulties
with a strange soprano. Travaglini and lons,
the bassi, come well through heavy tasks, also,

At Poverty Point.
Fred Belton used to relate, in Sydney, one of his early
London static experiences, with Mrs. Honey, the Nellie
Barren of her day. He had not long been married, and
was acting with her in the “Middy Ashore, where she
impersonated the Middy. She had to dress on top of a
flight of stairs, and implored him to carry her down, be-
cause she was afraid some of the bolt-ropes might give
way,
Madame Simonsen says the “Huguenots isa queeily
unlucky opera. Last season her Madame Cmti fell ill,
and she had herself to do Valentine, and this season Mdlle
Guidotti falls hoarse, and Boema has to be fetched tor
Valentine. But “Ernani” has from all time been re-
garded as the most luckless opera in Melbourne.
Melba is having her stage dresses built by Worth, of
Paris. And, bv the way, a London paper claims Melba
and Alhani as “two English artists.;’ Imperial
English, we presume, seeing that one is an Australian
and the other comes from Canada.
' Poor Win Robins, who plays the leading lady’s part in
the new London play “The Trumpet Call, had an
unpleasant experience on the first night. She has to
make a quick change of costume, but in the hurry o
dressing did not fasten her dress very securely. The
result was that, during a most pathetic scene, her skirt
became undone and down it dropped. Unluckily her
under-garments were few, she wore no petticoats in
point of fact she had nothing on but her “combinations,
so there she was facing the audience, winch, of course
set up a yell. It was an awtul moment, but the lady
preserved her sangfroid, and quietly pulled up her skirt,
fastened it and went on with the scene, amidst cheers,
as if nothing had happened. The next evening Beve-
ridsre before going on the stage, knocked at the lady s
dressing-room door and said, “Oh, Win Robins, are you
going to play this evening the same business you did
last night?”

A Sydney pressman recently received the following
polite epistle: “Dear Sir,-After some difficulty I have
ascertained that you are the contributor to the C
n theatrical column. A paragraph came under my
notice in last week’s issue, containing some remarks of a
personal character against a valued friend of mine (Miss
n ) Now, let me tell you this—that unless you insert
a few’words stating that the remarks were intended in a
locular strain, and not to be insulting, I shall take an
early opportunity of thrashing you m some public place. I
expect to see the apology, if not in this week s issue, at
any rate in that of the following.” How enviable is the
position of dramatic critic !

There has been a bit of newspaper paragraphing about
Henrv Irving’s loss of voice, all of which, no doubt, was
in the interests of that Prince of Cheap Advertisers
Morell Mackenzie. As a matter of fact Irving suffers
from a very slight affection of the uvula, which is apt to
enlarge with any sudden change of weather, and it has
been Considered advisable to use the knife This has
been magnified into “a delicate and difficult operation,
performed with much skill by that eminent specialist
s’j.. M m ” etc. The fact is that the operation is of the
simplest kind, and was performed on the writer by
Lennox Browne without any fuss or bother. All Browne
said was, “Now off you go, don t catch cold, and don t
drink spirits for - 24 hours.’

It has puzzled everyone-the non-success of “ Lady
Bountiful ” (says our London correspondent). It is one
of the sweetest plays ever written, was beautifully acted,
and needless to say, superbly mounted, and yet the
public hold aloof and could not be induced to look at it.
Is this a sign of the decadence of taste ? People flocked
to see “ Sweet Lavender,” yet this play, which is not the
same genre , fails to attract. All the while that rubbishy
drama, “The English Rose,” drew enormous houses.
In the new Adelphi drama by G. R. Sims and Robert
Buchanan there is a church-scene so suspiciously like the
scene of Lydgate Old Church in “Lady Bountiful” that
the critic of the Daily Telegraph drew attention to the
coincidence. Even the incident, an interrupted mar-
riage, is the same. Of his own accord Mr. Pinero has
come forward to acquit the authors of “The Trumpet
Call”—which is the title of the Adelphi play—of plagiar-
ism. Mr. Pinero states that immediately after the pro-
duction of “ Lady Bountiful ” at the Garrick, Mr. Sims
wrote to him and stated that a church-scene with a mar-
riage incident formed part of the story of the play upon
which he and Buchanan were then engaged. This makes
the coincidence all the more remarkable. In his letter to
the D.T. Pinero writes: “In justice to my friend, Mr.
G. R. Sims, and to Mr. Robert Buchanan.” Why this
distinction? Is not the aggressive Robert “a friend” of
Pinero’s?
Felix Tanner, who lives on an empty stomach, is going
to challenge Succi and Jacques to fast for the champion-
ship of the world. Tanner, by the way, had an all-round
shrinkage in Adelaide. He lost 36 lbs. in weight, 6 inches
round the chest, 7 inches round the centre, and his
banking account receded several inches.

The new Victoria Theatre, in Perkins-street, Newcastle
(N.S.W.), was opened by the MacMahon Brothers with
“ Evangeline ”on Saturday night last. It seats over 1300
people—s2o on the ground floor, 270 in the circle, and
550 in the gallery, and is owned by a syndicate. The
stage is 60ft. high, 66ft. in width, and 45ft. in depth.

lead. The company, strengthened by the engagement of
Blanche Elliott, formerly understudy to Frank Harvey,
the dramatic author, open at Bathurst on Friday for a
country tour.

Wretched stuff draws exulting crowds every Saturday
night at Melbourne Temperance Hall concerts—force of
habit and the profits must be vast. Dave Gardner fetches
on Noah’s Ark holus bolus, but you can’t help laughing at
the fellow. The lady-performers appear to come straight
away from the sewing-machine, without any pretence of
dressing.
Saturday Review mentions that a number of MSS. of
Chinese plays have been added to the British Museum.
One is very like “ Jane,” only better. Young Chi-ki has
been deceiving his rich uncle, Hoki Wang, by pretending
to be married, and, summoned on a visit to Nunky, in
another town, has to borrow' a wife from a friend. They
stop feasting so late that the town-gates are shut, and the
uncle insists on providing them with accommodation.

The Last Call G.M. Co. threatens the
Northern Miner with libel on .account of certain
sharp criticisms. The best answer that any gold-
mining company can make to nasty criticisms is
to hurry up and pay a big dividend out of crush-
ings, instead of wasting shareholders’ money on
legal “bluff.”
Masked balls are now all the fashion in
England. It is related that at one recently a
youth of ingenuous countenance and ingenuous
modesty was flirting the best he knew how with a
tall lady in a pink domino, when, to his great
astonishment and the no small amusement of a
few viveurs in the immediate vicinity of the pair,
the voice from behind the mask said : “Why,
Bobby, wherever did you learn such awful things?”
The pink domino happened to be his mother.
Mr. Steadman, the popular agent of the
A.M.P. Society, writes; —Ashburton Villa, 14
Campbell-street, Balmain, June 3, 1891.—Messrs.
Marshall Bros. —Dear Sirs, —Having heard so
many extolling the virtues of Marshall’s Tonic
Nervine, I think I should, unsolicited, add my
quota of appreciation to its excellence. For some
years past I have suffered at intervals from severe
attacks of nervous prostration and neuralgia, and
have invariably found that a bottle of this in-
estimable tonic has brought relief.—Yours faith-
fully, William H. Steadman.
Francis Adams, in the Fortnightly, on
Australian bushmen ;
Behold this native-born Australian of the interior, o*
the first and second generations, sullen and sombre-
souled, everlastingly confronting a hostile sun, his
digestion ruined with an endless stream of tannin (he
calls it tea), a spare eater, a spare drinker, pre-
cociously and neurotically amorous, biliously and satani-
cally proud, able to shoot rabbits and birds with a rifle,
and to sit any horse yet born from morning to night. He
cares so little for the curse of existence that on occasion
he will grinningly throw away his life for a trifle. When
he begins to blow or to swear his drawling words, super-
human lies, or face-whipping curses come out of him in
one long, nervous spasm. The English condemnatory
volubility is utterly gone. His generosity is reckless in'
its extremity. Touch his heart, and he tends you in your
hour of sickness like a woman in love. It is not for
nothing that one lives in desperate drought-land on
mutton, tannin and unspeakable damper.

A sign of the times is the fact that
Minister Garruthers (N.S.W.) is showing people
how all Yass belongs to one old lady, and that at
Goulburn and at Peel River 400' miles of
land and 54 miles of water frontage be-
long to an English, firm that had not a single
shareholder living in the province. Also that
in the Illawarra district the same state of things
existed, and that an enormous estate belonged to
two absentee Englishmen.
B. R. Wise's amending Bill imposing
municipal taxation on the unimproved value only
of land has set the N.S.W. property-holders by
the ears. A “ Suburban Alderman ” —probably a
member of the Single-Tax League, for the Single-
taxers run in all .shapes and under all sorts of
names—in the S.M. Herald, calls upon “all who
have a stake in this great land, and have at heart
the progress of this noble country,” to sit on such
rascality.

Lands that the Isis Downs run comprised 297
square miles of country which had been leased for
21 years, from January 1886, at a rental of fd.
per acre. That is to say, at £2 a square mile, or
for the whole of the immense area only £504 a
year, with a monopoly of 21 years, and the chance
of buying up “ the eyes ” of tile coluitry at the
lowest possible price.

The Colonial Sugar Co. is importing 500
Javanese to work its Goondi plantation, near
Geraldton, N.Q. About 18 months ago, a mere
handful of Javanese up there made a determined
figh t against the authorities, and as the total
armed force of the district is two constables, one
of whom is always absent on escort-duty, a rising
of 500 Javanese, in addition to those already on
the ground, would give the white men in the
district no end of trouble. These Javanese are
under a 3-years’ engagement, at 6s. 3d. per week
and rations !
King Billy, of the Macleay River, N S.W.,
now deceased, upon being asked what he must
liked in all the world replied : “Oh glassa rum,
my word!” “Now suppose you had the rum
what else would you like ? ” “ Bit bacca, I dhink.”
Being further interrogated as to wliat desire he
would next wish to gratify, Billy scratched his
head and replied : “Nudther glassa rum ray
pinny word.” This puts the whole nigger-vote
question into a nutshell. The nigger doesn’t want
a vote. He only wants rum and bacca. And
then more rum, and then some more bacca unto
seventy times seven.
On one Queensland run, 20,000 acres have
been selected at 10s. per acre. Treasurer
MTlwraith’s stone-broke policy is to sell cheaply
big areas to land-gobblers, and the colonists may
expect to see agents for secret syndicates acquiring
all the finest Queensland areas which the said

detriment of hona-fide men working for their oivfi
families. And MTlwraith’s estimate of the
year’s land-tevenue will probably be as far out
Were his calculations of his famous
tive, r/uasi-revenue tariff of 1888.
There are two Mackenzies in the Madfi-
land Legislature who hate each other with a dark
bloodthirsty, piratical hatred. Therefore, wheh
the Hansard officials sent the proofs of a speech
to the wrong Mackenzie by mistake, he waded in
to blast the other mail’s character for evermore
The things He made the other Mackenzie saV
that speech were calculated, had they been pub-
lished, to make hirii a thing of loathing and ah
Object of public scorn. Then at the last moment
the right Mackenzie got the proof, and when he
saw what he was alleged to have said his rage ivas
of a kind that only blood could appease.
Having declared recently that the Opposi-
tion was responsible for the time expended in
N. S. Wales Parliament in committee over the
“People’sßepresentation Bill,” the S.M. Herald
the other day somersaulted in a leader, ahd
ehaeavbiired to put the entire blame on the
shoulders of the Labour-party. Had the Herald
honestly desired to expose the culpable individual
it Would have stated that the Premier who intro-
duced such a crude, bomplbx and ill-constructed
measure to the House was solely responsible.
Palkes never had any constructive genius or any
capacity for selecting subordinates it) possession
of the faculties lacking in himself.
Mitchelson, M.P., of Maorilaud, has got
about the surest seat in all Australasia. The last
three men who ventured to contest his consti-
tuency all died by violence almost immediately
afterwards—two of them by suicide—and now
anybody who wants to live gives Mitchelson a
walk-over. The two suicided individuals both
went the same way; a blight, or a curse, or a car-
buncle seemed to come over them immediately
after the election, and they pined away, and went
mad, and then they softly deposited their brains
on the floor and died. Still even Mitchelson can't
last for ever. Some day, we suppose, he will lose
his seat, and then he will probably hang himself
on the door-knob in the Parliamentary refresh-
ment-room, and the tragedy will be finished.
It is stated by certain enemies of the
N.S.W. Labour-party, that in one of the Sydney
city electoral labour leagues, several hundred
members voted by ballot for the nomination of
a certain bunch. The ballot-papers were ap-
parently 0.K., but when an effort was made by
post to collect the “ sustentation ” fees, only
about 100 living members of the league could be
found. The votes received by post, which deter-
mined the choice of candidates, were those of
absent men, dead men and myths. The rolls, in
fact, had been stuffed, and there can, of course,
be no identification of the ballot-papers, when all
the free and independent voted anonymously per
mail. And similarly in regard to another elec-
torate.
Gillies lured the Victorian Government
into a tight place the other night, and then crept
silently away to revel in his saponaceous smile.
It was at Duncan's instigation that Premier
Munro enlarged a schedule of proposed expendi-
ture on public buildings, with the result that
many of the “ins” voted against the amended
list, whilst only a few of the “ outs ” were included
in the Government’s narrow majority. Deakin
and Pearson (be it noted) supported Munro’s
inane attempt at pleasing the other side, but
Dow, Wrixon and J. B. Patterson voted as hard
as they could against their own policy of twelve
months ago. _ Gillies supported neither side, but
simply remained true to his secret principles.
When_ the muffin-bell rang the leader of the
< )pposition was numbered with the missing. He
had retired to some far off pub. for a drop of Scotch
whisky and a snigger. It was a fair average
illustration of how the old and artful variety of
politician serves his country.

Old Adye Douglas, of Launceston (Tas.), a
clever lawyer, and yet an honest man, is the
youngest-looking man for his age on this side of
the Line. Adye must be considerably over 100,
yet might be taken for 50. He has been Premier,
Convention Representative, and Agent-General
for his province, and has had as many wives as
Blue Beard.

Phil. May is now universally recognised
as being in the top rank of the world’s great black-
and-white artists, who may be numbered on the
fingers Of one’s hands. His success in Europe is
only bounded by Ins health, which is uncertain.
An Australian lately-returned from England saw
a letter addressed to May by the editor ot the
Graphic, who, alluding to some of Phil s sketches,
said that he “had seen nothing which had im-
pressed him so much since his poor friend, Calde-
cott, first astonished them all.

John Alger, of Alger’s wharf, Sydney, is,
like George Thornton, of somewhat uncertain age.
Ear back, in the fifties, he was a sort of standing
dish at parties. It was the custom 111 those primi-
tive davs to propose toasts at supper, and John,
like George Reid to-day, was always called upon
to return thanks for the ladies. To look at the
old fellow now with his hoary beard, one can
hardly realise the fact that he was ever a smart
ladies’ man. One of his daughters was married,
the other day, to a man with a donble-baxreUeal
name —manager of a branch bank at Rensington
(London.)
In a Southern province, the grandson of a
.Commander of the Forces (colonial) is a working
miner, and the foreman of his shift is a son of a
Rear-Admiral; a chain-man in a neighbouring
surveyor’s gang is a grandson of a former Governor,
and a boat hand on the steamer which brings
supplies to the mine is a first cousin of the present
Governor of another Australian province, while
the caretaker in the boat’s-office at the home port
is the eighth son of another Governor. Every
man Jack of the lot had, at one time, an indepen-
dent income, and every one has “ dossed” in
No. 2 Boiler, Cole’s Wharf, or similar institutions.
Let us not sneer at Monaco or its prince,
who has alonger pedigree than any other potentate,
great or petty, in Europe. Fqr more than eight
centuries the title passed down a direct line qf
males. It was net until 1731 that a daughter qf
the house had to -take up ,the running, and ,tbe
present Prince is her great grandson. A revolu-
tion in the little Principality would cause thirty
.deceased .sovereigns, all .of the same blood, qr
politely supposed Jo have been, Jo tqrn in their
coffins There never was such another family for
nursing the same old billet from generation to
generation. No idea of work has coursed through
.their delicate blue veins for ; a thousand years.

The same skd, sorrowful* old story; ThO
ollief sufferers in the Bank of Van Diemen’s
Land smash are lone widows, poor orphans, ahd
impoverished old ladies, and this while certain
high officials and particular pets were allowed to
overdraw to the tune of £lO,OOO, £20,000, and even
up to £52,000!

To give some idea of how recklessly the
old ship was allowed to drift on to the rocks, the
overdraft account of one new customer of the
V;D.L. Bank was opened with a 2s. (id. debit for
a pass-book !

A Waiter of the Australia iJotel, questioned
as to Sara’s alleged marriage with Darmont, said,
sagely—“ Well, we didn’t know of it, and you’d
think a thing like that would leak out somehow—
but, mind yet; there Was a terrible lot of priests
hanging round her for the last day or two before
she sailed.'’ It looks as though the interesting
event came off. Sara would naturally require a
terrible lot of priests to prepare her for a rentree
into the married state.
# # *
Yachts are expensive things to keep.
Even the solid millionaires of .Sydney dabble
little in them. Only the officials of unchartered
banks can afford such luxuries.
* * #
A lot of fuss is being made over the case
of Barnett, or Burnett, the ricketiy Sydney crank
who, after being ordered to gaol for three years
for indecent offences, was released (on an official
medical certificate that he had heait-and-lung
disease and couldn’t live long) only to commit
similar offences again. But who ever heard
more than a whisper about the release of
the wealthy Petersham (Sydney) alderman
who got seven years for forging the name of
a dead man, and a year or so afterwards was
seen calmly driving round in a nobby buggy
behind a spanking trotter ? It is averred, by the
way, that prior to his release —on what ground
it was assented to has never been stated —that
municipal magnate parted £lOOO to a denomi-
nationally-controlled charitable institution.
* * •
The objection urged on behalf of the public
to the hearing of the divorce-cases in camera,
remindsus of at leastonecelebrated Australian case
which was heard in open couit, notwithstanding
the position of the parties. Sir Robert Moles-
worth, Judge of the Supreme Court cf Victoria,
sought a divorce from his wife (both now dead) in
consequence of her alleged misconduct with,
amongst others, the late R. D. Ireland, Q.C. A
good deal of the case was mere suspicion, and
although tried before a “brother Judge” the
petitioner did not succeed. Dawson, who appeared
for Mrs. Molesworth, was unsparing in his satire
on the old Judge. J. D. Wood, who appeared for
Ireland, asked his client, in the witness-box, if
“on his honour,” he ever had any criminal
intimacy with Mrs. Molesworth. “Dick” placed
his palm over the region of his heart, and with
great solemnity declared “on his honour ” that he
never had. J. D. Wood is the only one now left
who took part in the case.
* * *
A certain lawyer who contested a N.S.W,
constituency at last general election went to the
women’s type-writing rooms and offered them all
his work if they would give him 50 per shent dis-
count — i.e., if they would write for a penny
a folio (twopence being the usual rate). The
affronted typists vow that they will make it hot
for that lawyer should he ever again dare to lift
up his voice on a public platform.
# # 8
Labour-delegate Dyson (N.S.W.) is of
opinion that barmaids are “either too proud or
too debased ” to help themselves, and can never be
unionised. lie thinks they should be abolished ;
During floocltime Condobolin (N.S.W.)
suffered several kinds of famine. The township
rations ran low and slush-lamps were the order of
the night. But “the most unkindest cut of all ”
was that the Condobolin fays ran out of hairpins,
and the local blacksmith was favoured with orders
to make patent skewers out of fencing-wire.
• * *
Recently, Cloncurry, N.Q., was without
any mails for three months on account of the
floods. The people there didn’t even know of
John L. Sullivan’s arrival!

* # *
Dear Bulletin, — He your doggerel on the
loquaciousness of Chinese wives and the conse-
quent penalty. There is a tribe of blacks in the
Northern Territory of S.A. who do not allow
their women to speak at all after marriage. This
law is strictly enforced, and obeyed, but I do not
know the penalty incurred by disobedience. The
gins above a certain age all converse by means of
signs. This tribe occupies the dry country
between the heads of the coast rivers running into
the Gulf and those falling inland, a sort of
debateable land between the coast and the in-
terior. In the ear ier days of the overland
telegraph-line an old man and his gin
came in to Powell Creek station and
were allowed to stay there. They got some
rations and a little tobacco, which they soon
learned to use, and did the usual blackfellow’s
work of bringing up the horses and milkers.
Nothing could induce the gin to utter a word. As
an experiment, her ra’ions and tobacco were
stopped, but it availed not. Tin n they stopped
the old man’s rations, and at last his tobacco.
This was too much for him, and ho sternly com-
manded her to speak. She did so : she turned on
her husband, and belaboured him with all the
long-pent curtain-lectures of years, all the bottled-
up abuse that should have been doled out in
instalments was paid in full. Next she went for
t;ie station-master, and then for all hands, wind-
ing up with the Chinese cook, who encountered
her valiantly in Chinese, and the two went at it for
hours. Finally, as she could not be stepped, she
had to be cast out, like a second Hagar, into the
wilderness. The aboriginal plan works well so
long as the rule is not infringed.— Delcomyn.
# * »
The Bulletin earnestly recommends the
foregoing facts to the attention of the Australian
women’s literary and debating societies.
The N.S.W. memorial to the late sculler
Searle, champion of the world, which was ordered
some time ago from some foreign country or other,
is about to be landed in N.S.W,, so that we shall
soon hear about an unveiling ceremony. A fig
for your public memorials ! After all the gush
and blather, they have not up to date raised quite
£lOOO as a memorial towards the chair of language
which is (or ought to be) established at Sydney
University in remembrance of bluff old Jack
Robertson. And how about the Dailey memorial,
concerning which there was such a flourish of
trumpets? And is Henry Kendall’s memorial yet
paid for? And how many years did they take to
raise a metal effigy of old Doctor Lang? Most of
our “ public memorials ” are gross shams. Look,
for instance, at one of the latest, the statue raised
at Melbourne to cranky Gordon, the demented
higger-slayer of the goUdah ! What does that
represent ? The nation’s appreciation of the
alleged virtues of the departed monomaniac? No !
If it really and truly represents anything more
than an advertising fake of a little Imperialistic
mutual - advertising society in and around- the
Argus newspaper office we should like to know.
* * »
The latest tumour about the Sydney daily
looming Protectionist paper, alleged to be (this is a
Very cautious paragraph) projected, is that it is to
be run chiefly by Victorians ahd with Victorian
capital. This somehow reminds one of the un-
happy first days of the Sydney Daily Telegraph,
Which was manned exclusively by Victorian re-
porters, who used, especially late at night, to write
about the “ Yarra” when they meant the Parra-
matta.
* * *
We crane our mental necks across the sea
in joyous expectancy of the new book, Sir Ber-
nard Burke’s “Colonial Gentry” Baronet
Clarke, son of rich Butcher Clarke and
so on. Is Parkes a gent ? Is Berry ? Is
Gillies ? London Daily News already
slashes up this nauseous muck. Wentworth
refused the baronetcy because he wanted to
be Lord Vaucluse. Sir Salmon Wilson gave
£40,000 to Melbourne University for a peerage,
which never came. But we may yet witness
an apotheosis of the podgy-raan with the flame-
coloured whiskers. His son (the umbrella
hero), it is cabled, is to marry a daughter (broken-
mouthed) of the late Duke of Marlborough. Thus
.will be united the two illustrious houses of the
hero of Blenheim and King Alfred the Great.

Verbatim extract from speech delivered at
the N.S.W. Art Society’s dinner, the other night,
about one a.m. : “ You talk about Lord Jersey as
a Governor ; my Gord, you say he’s the best we
ever had ; my Gord, I don’t think so; my Gord
no; I’ve known plenty of Governors, my Gord,
heaps of ’em, and they all said to me, my
Gord ” ——here the waiters turned the gas out,
and the sneaker and audience wobbled out into
the strept, fell down three times, and found them-
selves on their own door-steps.
« # #
The A.N.A. is doomed. It appears that
the champion goal-kicker of Victoria was born in
England, and the local Natives, who were about to
raise a testimonial for the young man and make
him perpetual president of their association, are
now of opinion that any association for which the
champion goal-kicker is ineligible is a disgrace tu
the country and ought to be abolished.
» * *
In St. Kilda (Melb.) Cemetery there is a
touching tombstone showing above the inscription
a design, in slight relief, of a hand holding a jug
at the angle one likes to see it held at on a warm
day. The stone was erected by a Mrs. to the
memory of her late husband, who kept a pub. and
was cut down like a flower in the act of filling a
jug of beer. _ He had just finished putting a head
on it with his right arm, “ the strong arm,” when
the awful summons came, and the beer, presum-
ably, began to slip from his nerveless grasp as
depicted on the tombstone.
* # #
Fourteen new names were added lately to
the roll of N.S.W. solicitors, which now numbers
considerably more than 600. Law is a very pay-
able industry if managed properly. Five leading
legal firms in Sydney net between £lO,OOO and
£20,000 each per annum, which is a tolerable
profit when you come to think that most of the
work is done by clerks. Apropos of the magni-
ficent salaries paid to managing clerks, a certain
Australian solicitor sometime ago engaged a
young English solicitor to conduct his business
(salary £l5O per annum) while he toured, the
Continent. The Englishman annexed £2OOO and
disappeared, and has never been captured although
a warrant was issued for his arrest. Last Christ-
mas the writer of this par came across the
quondam managing clerk at a big coffee-palace in
a neighbouring province where he held the
onerous position of “ boots.”

cherknow,

A Belfast (Vic.) parson has been thanking
God that he doesn’t belong to the class who
“ write for food and feed because they write.”
Which suggests one of two assumptions—either
that the parson can't get paid for his copy, or else
that he writes for drink.
ss #
Labertouche, secretary to the Vic. Rail-
way Department, doesn’t attempt to define what
constitutes a distinguished visitor in the free-pass
sense of the term. Its wide significance extends
from the globe-trotting duke to the sister of an
English soft-goods agent, who comes out with her
brother on one of his periodical business-trips.
The social status of a distinguished visitor is an
unknown quantity down to a certain point. It is
essential that he or she shall be
Hinglish, in comfortable circumstances, and well
able to afford the price of a ticket. Poverty
never travels on a free-pass,

Item from Sydney paper :
QYDNEY HOSPITAL.—WiII the Nurse to whom
kJ a gentleman raised his hat yesterday please
to enclose an address to “F.,” Daily Telegraph
■Office.
Very likely she will. And then if she is acute
and business-like nurse there will presently be a
ruined and insolvent “F.” lying about, and after
a few weeks of joy and bankruptcy he will gather
up the hat abovementioned, and steal away to
some place where he can cool on a damp hillside,
and the vanished smile of the cash that is dead
will never return again. For though we have
seen a good many advertisements from men who
wanted to get acquainted with a nurse, we have
never yet seen an “ad.” from one of them an-
nouncing that he had got rid of the nurse again,
and wasn’t acquainted with her any longer. The
newspaper is full of the footprints of the men who
go in, but there are no traces of the men who come
out again. They are financially eaten by the
nurse, we presume.
* # #
A certain Abraham Fogel, of Sydney,
lately saved 4s. Bd. by using obliterated stamps on
his receipts. He explained matters by stating
that he had fallen overboard in the Harbour with
the stamps in his possession ; but after it had been
pointed out that he wasn’t likely to meet anybody
under water who would take the stamps out of his
tail-pocket, and obliterate them with ink, and
then put them back again, he compromised by
paying (in accordance with the merciful verdict of
a jury which could have fined him about £600)
£7O. Dead loss on the transaction, £69 15s. 4d.
Thus is the old order of things stood upon its
head. Many a man has got 4s. Bd. from a person
named Abraham and paid £7O for it, but when
Abraham gets 4s. 8d and pays £7O, the end of
time and space is assuredly at hand, and the crack
of doom is coming with seven-league boots down
the loud-resounding corridor of history.
# * #
The British auxiliary squadron has not had
much of a welcome in Sydney. As it was fathered
by the Dibbs Government and not by Parkes, the
latter could hardly take much notice of it.

Oh, Dyson, would you sack the tart ?
And rob the dudelet of his hart ?
And break the baldhead’s thirsty heart?
Oli, Dyson !
Would’st shut saloons so private, snug,
Where fancy mashers barmaids hug,
And Bessie bilks the bashful mug?
Oh, Dyson !
Oh, D., this fatal deed think twice on,
For, ponder, £hou destructive Dyson,
Who then will serve thee with thy pisen ?
Oh, Dyson !
We love the barmaid, love to hang
Around her flies and hear her slang,
But most of all we love her bang,
Oh, Dyson !
THE FINE COLONIAL GENTRYMAN.
(A PICTURE FROM LIFE.)
He’s lord of half a million sheep,
And reaps a fleecy crop,
And when he isn’t fast asleep
He’s mostly “talking shop ;”
He never gave a pound away
Which didn’t bring him two ;
His parents were sent out, they say.
Aboard the Jamberoo —
This fine Colonial Gentryman,
One of our golden time !
He loafs around the “ Block M and smokes,
He’s loyal to the Queen ;
H e cannot see the point of jokes
Unless they are obscene;
He casts a small, suspicious eye
On all who haven’t “ stuff,”
And when the season’s extra dry
His adjectives are rough
This fine Colonial Gentryman,
One of our golden time !
He boozes in a brainless style
With kindred squatter men,
And buys a painted harlot’s smile
Just every now And then ;
He thinks the world is going wrong
When shearers make a stand ;
He’s sure the beggars’ souls belong
To owners of the land —
This fine Colonial Gentryman,
One of oitr golden time !j
He tries to ape the heavy swell,
But cannot judge the “side
However, he does something well—
He knows the way to ride.
Then let us drain a bumper to
Dame Nature’s dullest work,
The “ lag’’-begotten Johnnie who
Has got his name in Burke,
As a fine Colonial Gentryman,
One of our golden time !
Sir Salmon set his heart
On being made a Bart.,
And, yet, no matter bow he neatly set it,
That Salisbury, Tory chap,
Eluded Salmon’s trap,
And, darned if he could ever hope to get it.
Not Hughenden, the den
Of dear, departed Ben,
Could work the point as he was seeking arter;
But now his eldest son
Will quickly get it done -
They’ll “ Bart-ify” the hub of Marlbro’s darter!

joints. I tried nearly every known remedy, and
received but little relief until I commenced to use
the MICROBE KILLER.

can say that I have not felt so well for eight or
nine years, and can now do my work as well as
ever I could, and also enjoy my meals, which for
many years I could not.—l remain, yours thank-
fully ’ THOS. FISHER

There was an interesting point about a
waees case, tried the other day at the other end
of the world. At Westminster County Court,
Miss Chappel, house and parlourmaid (a sign of
the times was that she is spoken of by the
naners as “Miss” instead of Sarah or Mary),
sued a Mr. Kennedy lor nine days’ wages at 16s.
‘ wee k Sixteen shillihgs a week for a house-and-
narlour’maid in England! She bad been dis-
charged by her master without wages for refusing
td wear a cap. Defendant urged that it was the
recognised custom for servants to wear caps. The
court decided otherwise, and Mr, Kennedy had to
pay up- # *
R. J. Whelan, in it description of a 1000-
Ihiles ride across Queensland, tells how he had as
companions for the first part of the journey two
voung fellows bound for Normanton—one return-
ing to a young wife after six months’ absence, and
the other going to get married. The rivers were
a p bankers, but this ardent pair compelled
Whelan to dash through all obstacles. At Vena
Park he parted with them, but afterwards heard
that they rode four horses to death and were fined
at Normanton for furious driving.
Dr. Richard West-
rum, who died at .Syd-
ney recently, from a
self -administered
over-dose of morphia,
was a typical German
who loved science.
He had many friends,
and was esteemed
everywhere as a good
■surgeon and a good
fellow. The circum-
stances of his death
were peculiarly pain-
ful. A couple of
vears back he fell out
of a buggy and hurt
his knee. The injury
remained, and it was
discovered that the
joint was tuberculous.
This effectually lamed
him, and he took to
his bed, in which he
lay in one position
for at least fifteen
months. Meanwhile,
tuberculous lung-
disease progressed
rapidly, and it was
to end his terrible
suffering, which, as a
medical man, he knew
could only be termin-
ated by death, that
he took the morphia.
The doctor was
formerly a house-
surgeon at Sydney
Hospital, and it is a
ghastly fact that he
was the last of four
resident doctors who
left that institution
in succession each
to die of phthisis !
Thequarters provided
forthehouse-surgeons
were, until the advent
of Dr. Hull, the pre-
sent medical superin-
tendent, a perfect
death-trap. There is
a lesson in sanitation
for an incredulous
public. The saddest
part of this sad busi-
ness is that not one
out of the four dead
young doctors was a
eons titutionally-
phthisical man.
# * *
Dr. Beehag, the
clever young medico
who died of typhoid
at Sydney the other

# # *
The Melbourne
Turn-Yerien brought
off their spectacular
annual, the mask
ball, at Melbourne , .
Exhibition, the other evening, with much
brilliancy. The symbolic pageant was capital.
There were two big trophy-cars. One enshrining
the king and queen of the carnival, the other bore
six fine girls typifying the Australian provinces.
# # *
The representation of Parkes in the
Federation tableau at the mask ball aforesaid,
was greeted with roars of laughter. This was
rather hard, considering that the Germans in-
tended to do honour to the Hairy Bismarck,
whose snowy locks ■ and stately mien, combined
with an old-fashioned nail-can and black-green
suit, occupied a great deal of time and attention
in their get-up.
At the recent annual dinner of the British
Authors’ Society, Mona Caird refused to rise
When the Queen’s health was proposed, and her
example was followed by two or three other ladies
of less note. Lord Monks well was chairman of
the clinrier; and in giving permission for the
customary post-prandial smoke, he laid especial
stress upon the word gentlemen. Notwithstanding
this broad hint, several ladies produced cigarette-
cases and lit up. The German habit of smoking
during meals is gaining ground in England, like
everything Teutonic. Oscar Wilde always puffs a
cigarette after soup and after game.
# * *
The Age devotes a column article to tlih
verbal inventions of America —without including
the now sacred name of “ masher,” a word which
will probably live as long as there ate fellers and
girls in the land.
4 * *
“ I devise and bequeath £5OO a year to
my wife, unless she marries again ” —is about the
pinnacle of selfishness. As if it mattered a whiff
Of brims tone-vapour to a ghost stewing in its own
gravy whether its earthly relict took unto herself
another protector Or another sponger. Until the
day of reform comes when the State will deal
with all estates of deceased persons, it would not
be half a bad idea to make null and Void all
testamentary clauses conducive to open or Secret
immorality.
One of the so-called “great Australian
illustrated weeklies ” last week contained not a
solitary original picture!
James Smith, whose soul was supposed to
be above flunkey journalism, supplies the Argus
with a two-column account of some oil-paintings
“which grace the walls of the well-lit and well-
proportioned picture-gallery at Woolooraanata,
the country residence of Mr. F. W. Armytage.”
A shorter and brighter sketch of a real gallery-
one contaming better works and more of ’em—
would be printable for one of the London society
papers upon which James Smith visits his vener-
able contempt.

Steamship Company bringing a libel-action

The party whose ideas seldom soar higher than a
plain joint in private life will grumble at t ic
turtle-soup, and pass scathing criticisms upon t e
dessert, When he’s afloat in a first saloon.

A female witness in an Ipswich (Q.) Court
begged to be allowed to be sworn on her own
Bible:—“l’m a teetotaller and I’ve taken a
solemn oath that ‘ the lips that touch whisky shall
never touch mine;’ likewise, that the mouth
that tobacco’s defiled shall not kiss the mouth of
this child;’ and, I know, your Honor, that
many previous witnesses who drink and who
smoke have polluted the Court Bible, 1
couldn’t abear to have my bps polluted. me
maiden’s prayer was granted, and her breath still
smells only of strong essence of ginger and eau de
Cologne and almond dew and other essential oils
dissolved in pure rectified spirit about £■> o.p.
Apropos, the chief sister in a suburban hydney
Good Templars’ Lodge is a daughter of the
publican who sells the most—and the best-
whisky locally . „

What the Sydney Corporation could not
effect, the directors of the Hotel Australia did.
Martin-place is to be open for vehicular traffic.
Henry Gorman is chairman of the directors.

The Salvation Harmy people in Melbourne
have chartered the Wairarapa (N.Z. Co.) to carry
a hallelujah picnic crowd down to Port Phillip
Heads and take General Booth from off the Tas-
manian boat. £llO for a special steamer seems
very like vanity. There used to be none of these
naval demonstrations on the lake of Galilee.

Beaney’s sale— that of his earthly Para-
dise, at Cromwell House—last week drew almost
as big a crowd of the women as Evangelist Grubb.
What struck people most was the prodigious pro-
vision for eating and drinking, under Beaney’s
regime and regimen.
# *
Wollongong (N.S.W.) is running a mid-
night ghost of its own. The spook is not proof
against road-metal, well and truly aimed, but fled
from a volley with shrieks infernal.
# * *
On the last voyage of the Warfego from
Melbourne northwards a bright little maiden
made a surprising sensation on board, and broke
the moral bank of the community. She was
pretty, and aged about 18, and she was travelling
alone—therefore the male passengers set her down
as an orphan going out to face the cold, hard
world by herself, and they looked after her
tenderly." But the second night out, just as the
ladies were beginning to enjoy the moonlight, and
their husbands and brothers were organising the
customary poker-party, there was a burst of wild
melody and a flapping noise somewhere On deck;
Then there was a sound like somebody drawing a
cork in a secluded corner behind one of the deck-
houses, and a sudden howl, and a bit of an opera;
It was the bright little maiden, and she Was out
on an unholy toot with a bottle of brandy. A
motherly old female went and remonstrated with
her amid the shadows, but she said she
wouldn’t go home till morning, and asked
the old lady to “ come-an’-’avedrink ” ! Then she
broke loose and fled along the deck to the smoking-
room, where she offered to marry the entire poker-
party and assured them that they were jointly
and collectively the only man she had ever loVed.
Having finished this statement she embraced
three of them and breathed into their ears. Here
the captain and two officers took a hand and
gently shifted her below, and as she went she pro-
posed to the three of them. After which the
stewardess confiscated the brandy-bottle and
locked the erring girl in an empty berth for the
night, and left her calling for drink in her dreams.
The poker-party broke up early that evening ; it
consisted mostly of elderly gentlemen who hadn’t
been proposed to by a bright maiden of 18 for
many years, and the shock was too much for
them.
# * *
At Orange, N.S.W., on 11th inst., every-
body wore his Sunday suit and his best girl, just
to see a water-fountain unveiled in Cook Park, by
Mrs. Dalton, the wife of the donor, who after-
wards gave a champagne luncheon to a select few,
while the common variety of Granger had a free
tap of water :
Wear a black coat and hold your tongue
and the earth is yours.—Bulletin Philosophy,
book 38, page 796.
'# * *
Here’s a model servant-girl, aged 18.
According to an Australian lady-writer (a parson’s
wife, of course) in last Westminster Review :
“ She used to milk 20 cows on Sunday morning,
walk 10 miles to church and 10 miles back, then
milk the 20 cows again.”
# * *
Old High School (Tas.) boys will learn
with regret that their Alma Mater is to be closed
for want of support —it has never done any good
under its new regime and new title of “ Christ’s
College.” They" were jolly old days in the time
of the good old Rector, “Mother Hunt,” and the
soup riots.
* * *
Hobarb Amateur Gaiety Society, really a
fairly creditable dramatic club, threatens to break
up, because some “ toney ” members whose sisters,
cousins and aunts ivere in trade, won’t act with
other people’s sisters, cousins and aunts who are in
trade ! So Manager Hudspeth tears his hair and
dances on his Sunday hat.
* * *
The ruling passion of Young Australia is
a “ presentation group” of photographs in a large
frame. In fact, the ordinary athletic club, or
small mutual-improvement society, is formed with
the main idea of enabling the members to present
the president with a collection of portraits,
mounted and set off by a Dutch-metal margin.
This demoralising practice wants putting down in
the interest of jewellers’ shops. All presentations
should take the form of leaden silver-ware, charged
as best electro in the bill.

The Australasian Critic, edited by a
Melbourne University professor or two, has gone
bung. The post-mortem shows it to have died of
haw-haw.

THE GREAT REMEDY FOR PAIN.
ST. JACOBS OIL.
WHAT IT IS.
SIX STANDARD VIRTUES.
Ist. It is in one word a cure ; it is not merely
a relief, and in no sense a cure-all; it is the
product of scientific research.
2nd. It strengthens while it sooothes and sub-
dues, heals and cures ; it literally conquers pain.
3rd. Its effects are curative and permanent to
the whole group of muscular miseries and nervous
agonies.
4th. It does not merely irritate the outer sur-
face, nor does it merely soften or relax a constricted
muscle. To its specific action a superior curative
virtue is superadded.
sth. It penetrates deeply but gently; search-
ingly and surely seeking the pain spot in an effort
to conquer.
6th. _ Each constituent of the formula has a
recognised intrinsic virtue to serve most surely
the cure of pain.
WHAT DOES?
What becomes of the word after it is spoken?
Also, what becomes of the light when the candle
is blown out? .Both these queries were gravely
propounded for discussion some years ago in the
Debating Society of a country township in
A' ictoria by a member who was an earnest seeker
after truth. Both subjects provoked a considerable
deal of unpleasantness, which in one instance, at
least, culminated in physical violence, and the
removal of a highly respected stock and station
agent, who was a God-fearing man with one leg
shorter than the other and a lar,ge family, to the
local hospital for repairs. But they were never
satisfactorily solved, and ip the course of the effort
some neighbors ceased to be friends; relatives
even became temporarily estranged, and one
estimable family of Polish origin and the Hebrew
persuasion became so broken up that it could
never again be united. The wife went to her
mother, the husband went on the roads with
jewellery, the son stole all the horses off the town-
ship reserve and headed for Queensland, and the
daughter went to service. This is a true story,
and all brought about by a crank, who suddenly
disappeared and was never seen for years until he
turned up a little while ago in the same locality
as a book canvasser. And now he is dead.
W Inch is of no consequence except as a subject
whereon to congratulate everybody in all the
colonies wherein he might have strayed at some
time had he lived. The only question'on the same
lines that has never been answered is—“ What
becomes of all the people who are annually blown
up by kerosene explosions ?’’ Of course, any
answer to that must be pure guesswork, though
some of them who have come down again have
promptly guarded against a repetition of the
incident by purchasing Luxene, the new illu-
rninant, from one or other of the undermentioned
firms : Messrs. J. Barlow and Co., T. W. Eady
and Son, Mason Brothers, Limited, D. Mitchell
and Co., 1 illock and Co., J. Keep and Sons,
Holdsworth, Macpherson, and Co.. M‘Lean
Brothers and Bigg, Limited, Fred. Butcher,
Robert Miller, and J. F. Barrett, Sydney; J.
Ireland, R. Hall and Sons, and David Miller,
Newcastle. But with regard to those whenever
return the query still awaits solution. What does
become of them ?
AN APT QUOTATION.
“ Suit the action to the word, the word to the
action.”— Shakespeare.
The word is “ Waterbury,” the action comprises
the insertion of the digits into that receptacle of
your habiliments where you conceal the proceeds
of your daily toil and the prompt withdrawal of
13s 6d. This sum will procure a Waterbury watch
series E, a faithful and reliable time-recorder ; or,
if you can afford to dip a little deeper into your
pocket and part with 22s 6d, invest in a series J
or L. These are marvels of mechanical and
inventive ingenuity.
The J is a short-wind, stem-setting watch, with
quick-train, back-racket, jewelled duplex escape-
ment, sunk seconds-hand, enamelled dial, and
dust-proof case. The new ladies’ Waterbury
(series L) is a similar watch to the above, made in
a smaller size and without a second-hand. It is a
neat and elegant little article, and, above all, an
accurate time-keeper.

A Parisian has been sent to gaol for in-
sulting the executioner: “ There goes the assassin!”
he shouted. Which reminds one that good old
oir Allred btephen once strongly advised the
oydney hangman— so says that grim functionary—
to take a libel-suit against Sydney Evening News,
which had spoken of the finishing-schoolmaster in
disparaging terms. In our mind’s eye, Horatio,
see that alleged interview between Sir
Alfred and Jack Ketch. What an historical
picture it would make !
Before a French correctional tribunal the
. V a y a woman was charged with marital
infidelity, her paramour being one Bodin, also
arraigned. The husband proved his ease and
then asked for mercy on behalf of Bodin. “ Ah,
Monsieur le President,”said he, “you don’t know
Justine ! She is a regular demon, and has been
the scourge of ray life ever since I married her.
You cannot imagine what a relief it is to be rid of
her. Bodin, poor man, has taken my burden upon
himself. He little knows wjiat is in store for
him. (Shrieks of laughter.) The Court gave
Justine six days’ gaol, and allowed poor Bodin to
escape with a fine of IOOf. only.
The Lord Mayor of London was lately
made a baronet for entertaining the German
Emperor. “ The entertainment cost the Mayor
very little and the public a great deal,” says Moon-
shine. “ The public cannot have a baronetcy, of
course ; still, in recognition of its share in the
affair, we do not see why a baronetcy should not
be conferred upon the city rate-collector.” This
is the soundest argument we have read for a long
time in any Tory paper,
, This is the manner of sub-contract as she
is practised in Queensland, West Australia, ay,
and N.fe.W. for that matter. A takes a big rail-
way contract and lets it out in hunks to sub-con-
tractors. B and C take from A a fairly large
job at a price per cubic yard that doesn’t nearly
cover the cost of construction. B and C have
not a cent between them with which to pay
wages, but they have a notion and they know how
to home-make grog. So they run up a kennel
which they call a boarding-house, and on the
strength of their contract get credit from the
butcher, baker, and storekeeper. Then they
engage men, but only such men as agree to board
at their hash-house and patronise deeply their
home-made fuse Toil. By and by, as the contract
is about finished, B bolts to Guam with all the
cash A pays him, slipping up his wages-men and
the storekeepers as well, while C stays behind,
pretendedly cursing B, goes insolvent, gets for-
given and generally pitied. Then C combines
with D to take another contract, and plays the same
old game, at the end of which it is C’s turn to slope
and join B in Guam. The law should deal
strictly with sub-contracts, by at least making
the chief-contractor responsible for all wages.
Let the chief protect himself against the sub. by
special agreement and the withholding of pay-
ments to the sub. until the latter’s men’s wages are
paid and duly receipted.
E. G. GOERTZ,
(Member Brisbane Stools Exchange),

The Pacific “Slope,” or The Missing
Bank Manager.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The most necessary qualification for a suc-
.f.ii bank official, in these business times, is a
Sough comprehension of the twin arts of ' lying
f °!> and “levanting.” At the first blush it
«muld appear that the would-be levanter has no
Sce-or, at least, a very slender chance-of
caning from this island continent, seeing that he
f! watched at every port, and on every outward-
bound vessel, by an army of heaven-born detec-
ts and lynx-eyed and inspired police, who are
Clairvoyants at seeing, and trance-mediums at m-
Snreting “a clue.” Think of the complex
organisation arrayed against the solitary, and
Cften unaided, attempting absconder ! His des-
C- Dtion is recorded at every police-station in
J,. v province, but as the description is invariably
!! unlike the “ wanted ” as human blundering can
mke it, perhaps that dosen’t matter much;
housh on the other hand, as the average bobby
always reads the description upside-down and m-
Cde out, perhaps it does matter after all. His
photograph is largely circulated amongst the sea-
port officials, but, as he generally alters his facial
appearance, this also has to be discounted. The
free and urgent use of the telegraph wires is avail-
able to the whole body of thief-catchers, and any-
except a balloon can be stopped anywhere
cn route “on suspicion.” It is owing to the com-
nlpteiiess of police organisation that the occasional
cultures, which the public are taught by the press
to regard as “ sensationally smart,” are made, but
there are nothing more than the ordinary
seauences of the driest routine work. Ihe police
nfficial —the “D” or the common constable—
always moves in fixed grooves. His “information
received ” is the result of the treachery of a
“fiz°ig” or criminal traitor in his pay often a
less offender whom he could arrest if he chose,
hut whose peccadilloes he ignores in consideration
of his “ peaching ” services; still more frequently,
a ladv of shadowy repute who has, as her tempor-
arv husband, a criminal, or an associate of
criminals. Once get outside of police routine and
the whole service is at fault. A “ wanted who
knows the official methods needs only money and
cool assurance to circumvent them.
# * *
I could tell—and I will tell some other
time if The Bulletin thinks fit—a strange,
weird yarn of a successful “lying low ’ and a
subsequent clean “levanting,’’the peculiar feature
of which was that the “wanted was taken
aboard an outward-bound vessel m a police-boat,
and by the very police-officer who held the
warrant for his arrest, and who was going to
search that vessel for him. I could—and may
possibly-tell another anecdote about another
wanted, who hid for many weeks, and who fed
and “grogged” sumptuously every day in a
government building, under the very noses of the
police of a large city ; but the poor devil got so
sick of his retirement that he at last gave himself
up and faced the resultant music.
I have been led to think of thtse things from the
receipt of a letter from a former friend of mine,
who went wrong and is at present at Honolulu
for the benefit of his health. The letter of this
—I am sorry to say unrepentant man—shall speak
for itself :
Honolulu, Sunday, 00th, X —, 1891.
My dear , You will see by the above heading that *
am in safety enjoying the fruits of my big coup. lam
satisfied, thank you. I have gained the ambition of my
life. When I look back at my chances, as some one said
once (was it Clive or Warren Hastings?), I am astonished
at my own moderation. It was a work of years. I was a
pious little messenger-boy, and very lucky on race horses.
1 was a steady and a careful book-keeper, with the devil's
own fortune at fan-tan. I made a splendid confidential
clerk and learned some “ points’' from the directors, who
insisted on explaining to me the weaknesses in the exist-
ing bank laws. They didn’t want those laws meddled
with, however, because though there were many holes by
which the bank fell through, there were still more holes
which let the customers through, and a general repair
would have left the institution in the lurch. When I was
promoted to be manager of a branch I knew that my
chance had come. I did the confidence trick in the old,
old way—the Sunday-school racket and the church-choir
enthusiasm. I needn’t put you up to my methods of
‘‘ general scoop.” Should 3 r ou ever get a billet iii a bank,
though, I’ll send you out the fullest rules and regulations
to guide you on your royal progress to wealth.
How did I get away ? As simply as taking a tram. I
trusted no one except Bill Rogers. I trusted him because
he would have been compromised himself if I bad been
cotched. I did not even* confide in my wife and family.
I had to get them a distance away on a complicated yarn
that wouldn’t interest you ; it was very easily invented. Bill
and I soon came to the conclusion that the safest way to
“ lie low ” until the “ hue and cry ” was a hit abated, was
as an invalid in the house of a policeman in a marine
suburb. Bill knew of just such a party—a thick-headid,
goodnatured Irishman, described in the evening papers
Is a “ very intelligent officer.” “To be coorse ” (he said
in reply to Bill) “ I will oblige yere frind, th’ sick malm,
an’ it’s the purty shpare room Oi have, an’Molly s the
kindest craythur that iver drew the breath av loife.
And so I lay for many days in the calm seclusion of the
official bosom, shamming sick, being visited almost dary
by the local doctor, and letting ray beard grow (I only
had a moustache before) and only going out on dark
nh-hts for brief exercise. The weeks sped on, and as my
whiskers began to form a natural framework of disguise
around iuy features, I thought it was time to make the
next move. So I gradually recovered-to some
extent-and one blazing morning I hade tie
policeman and his wife a sad and tender faie
well, and departed feebly with a view to completing my
recovery in the mountains. Then I made for the loneliest
place on the adjacent sea-shore, and having bathed my
face and hands, neck and forearms, in lemon-,puce, I lay
drying in the sun on the glittering sand. The transfor-
mation was wonderful. In two hours I was like a half-
baked nigger. I lay low in my new capacity all day in
the hush, and that evening Bill Rogers fetched along a
romdi suft of clothes, a swag and a hilly, and a soft telt
hat? I started on the wallaby taking the south-east
coast I turned up at the new Pamhula diggings where I
opt work on a claim for a time- navvy work. I soon
sickened of the eternal pick and shovel, hut it was
necessary as part of my sate plan. After a few weeks on
Pamhula I went on to the seaport of Eden, in company
with other returning prospectors. I was only one among
a dozen bronzed diggers (the lemon-juice trick had been
repeated several times), and I caused no suspicion. 1 got
away to Hobart-pretendedly en route for the Zeehan
silver-mines—by a small steamer, and the rest was easy—
an unsuspected and open voyage to this joyous island,
where everybody is either a happy nigger or an absconder^
I send you these particulars in case, dear bov, you may
ever need to skedaddle suddenly. Enc osed are ten £2O-
- that I omitted to change into gold before I lef t > t
are of no use to me here. Their numbers are not know n
to the bank. Good bye.—Yours at rest. Bill Bolt.
I forward this letter for publication, for the
benefit of any Bulletin readers who may need to
“ slide ” surreptitiously. Also, to give the polioo
a hint.—Yours, &e., The Dipsomaniac.

Who on carlh is the “ Bone Idler, who
drivels so drearily in every Saturday s Sidney
Morning Herald. The following, which was
picked up outside the Herald office the other
day, appears to belong to him (or her) .
Far away in the blue distance the yellow ocean stretches
in purple bars towards the creamy sandbanks, wind-
fluttered into golden columns. The sunset glory of the
heath-bloom shines blackly against the faint green sea-
weed lashing the sad tale of last summer against the
cliff’s dreamy ochre. Sad cattle meditatively waving tails
fly-tormented chew the end of digestive thought, dreamily
conscious of past calfdom, forgetful of shambles shading
the hopes that stand at the portal of hinted inimoitahtj.
Waving leaves of cacophonous bloom (lying through the
dim air breeze-laden eddying in hollow caverns where the
sad sea-voice speaks crass secrets and mutters strange
stories to the booming billows of indefinable time cresteel
with the foam-promise of eternity.
As the Bone-Idler sat splay-footed gazing at the sodden
sea-grass, pointed needle-like, and filled with soggy wool
cotton. Nature’s stocking darning itself into holes, out of
which started slimy rabbits burrowing marten nests in
* “You are too bad,” said the Young Person, “ you have
whittled my forefinger down to the bone do leave me a
■thumb—tea pouring for the tea-pot.” ....
The Bone-Idler closed bis pen knife with a sharp c ick.
“The thumb,” he observed, “is regarded as the symbol
of the victory of man over the animal creation—as sucli 1
resnect it.” Taking from his pocket the distinctive mark
of the difference between Chinese and European civilisa-
tions he said, “ Show me your finger. ’
She pointed to the ground foam scattered with ruddy
fragments. “ Pick them up,” said the Young Person.
“See’’said the Bone-Idler, “how Nature provides for
her children. Why interfere with her activities? The
ant will profit ’’—pointing to a fat bull-dog.
“ Bah,” said the Young Person, “it is time for tea.
In the Bourke (N.8.W.) Police Court the
other day a charge of gross cruelty to a camel
fell through because the lawyer for the defence
submitted that “a camel was not an animal
within the meaning of the Act, ’ and the Bench
concurred. This was the only defence that was
raised, therefore we presume it was the only one
available. Anyhow the cruelty seemed to be
proved with sufficient clearness, but evidently
that didn’t matter; anybody can tie up a camel
and build a tire under it on the strength of this
dcision.
The bar of Merry’s Royal Hotel, Croydon,
N.Q., is in the shape of a half-irioon ; its outer
space is 150 ft. in circuit and 32 T. in zone breadth.
Merry rather imagined the diggers of the first
Croydon rush knew the use of a bar. If the
above dimensions are correctly staged and ie
back-shelves of the bar form the diameter of the
half-moon, then, according to geometrical oscula-
tions the depth of the landlord’s part of the bar
is between 15 and 16ft., and covers an area of a
trifle more than 44i sq. yards (with counters);
while the public or drinking zone, winch is out-
side the counters and presumably clear standing
space, has an area of a fraction less than 3oi 2 sq.
yds. This, fr a wild bush hotel, should be baid
to beat. ,
A Socialism under ruling commercial conditions
—the Co-operative Irrigation Colonies.
Truth.
Truth, greatest of the virtues and rarest, has so
long been a stranger to ordinary commercial
business, that when a perfectly conscientious
man maintains even a well known fact, that tact
assumes an additional importance, ihe truth
that should be sacred is twisted, and turned, and
mutilated, and in many cases utterly suppressed,
to suit the commercial ends of enterprising
unscrupulous people. There are many of these
who will unblushingly steal facts relating to the
operations of others, of which they are only the
fraudulent and valueless representation. Looking
at the fact of truth’s scarcity in the proper light,
we must of a necessity value it the more because
of its rarity. And therefore, the following letter
from Father Fahy, of the Sydney (N • '' •»
Presbytery, speaks volumes for the efficacy of
Warner’s Safe Cure. Here is the reverend fathers
letter to 11. H. Warner & Co. “ Sydney, IS .S.W.
1 have heard Warner’s Safe Cure spoken of in the
highest terms, by men whose sole object was
truth, and to help their fellow creatures D. W.
Fahy, Catholic Priest. And the whole and
absolute truth about Warner’s Safe Cure is just
this ;—lt is an infallible cure in kidney and liver
diseases, and all the physicians of the advanced
schools believe that if the kidneys are kept pure
and workable, which only Warner’s Safe Cure can
effect, people would escape ninety per cent, ot
disease. This is the absolute fact, as thousands ot
persons in Australasia willingly testify.

On Satm-ctay last Mr. J. B. Clarke’s Stromboli
earned off the A. J.O. Derby after a desperate race with
Uxide, which latter animal got left in the second place by
a short head. The head was so short that if Oxide had
put out his tongue at the last moment he might
have won even then. The time was 2.41, and
0n u ,P n three occasions was the event run with
mo • iLJ* 00 *”!’ the shortest time being Trident’s
" 1,1 i°° 6 - ■“ter was third. Mr. George Hill’s Marvel
scooped the Epsom Handicap with a brilliant rush at the
last moment. Muriel, which sundry knowing individuals
declared to be the only possible winner of last year’s
Melbourne Cup, was second, just far enough off to lose,
and near enough to cause bitter execrations among those
who backed her at 16 to 1, and saw their money rise
hotore them like a dream and then fade away. Elymas,
? i ~v* 1 animal, was third, and sundry lavishly
backed brutes were nowhere. Both Stromboli and Marvel
were favounles at even money. The Hurdle Race was
taken by Bettma, a neglected outsider. Affiance, another
outsider, gathered in the Trial Stakes. The Spring
Stakes were carried off by Megaphone in brilliant fashion,
with Albemarle second, and that mysterious animal High-
born, which so gracefully lost last year’s Melbourne Cup,
close behind.

The great event of Tuesday was the Metropoli-
tan, which was won by J. Chaafe’s Yowi, in 3.33,
whereby no records were broken, neither was the Thames
combusted. Magnus, the favourite, got third place by a
desperate effort, and Sunshine was second. Most of the
public cash on this event was scooped in by the sons of
Jacob. Corvette annexed the Oaks in a hollow fashion
by four lengths. He was the favourite, and came in peace-
ruiiy at the head of a procession amid a solemn calm all
round.

Blundell Maple, the furniture man, has just paid, so the
cable says, £15,000 for Common, and £6,000 for a year-
ling colt by St. Simon. Evqo, Blundell Maple must be a
Juggins. It stands to reason-for instance, that the
owner of Common being very much in funds by the
horse s big successes, wouldn’t sell his hero if he thought
there was a reasonable chance of getting another fifteen
thousand (and interest) out of him. Common, moreover
was never brought out as a two-year-old, and has only run
four times this season. A delicate animal, no doubt, that
couldn t make Surefoot gallop at weight-for-age. It looks
as though Maple’s head requires furnishing.
* # #
On Uanda Station, in West Queensland, a sun-
downing American nigger and the cook at the men’s hut
managed to brew a putupyerbloominhandser. In the
fourth round the cook cleverly caused the darkey to sit
down in a soft, squashy, 200 deg. Fahrenheit, half-baked
and well-baking damper concealed in some lovely white-
hot ashes; This unexpected poultice applied to the
other end ’ terminated the fight amid howls and mad
boundings towards the roof of the hut. It was a master-
piece of calculated art, and beats all the throat blow-s of
Slavm and the heart prods of Sullivan. It will be knowm
in the ring in future as “The Cook’s Finisher.”

and oons, of George street, Sydney, have been ap-

Arthur Rae, one of the members for the
Murrumbidgee, has given notice of the following
resolution, which he proposes to move in the
iN.S. W. Legislative Assembly on 20th October
That in the opinion of this House no form of federation
will prove acceptable to the people of N.S. Wales which
does not provide for the complete political independence
ot the Australian Commonwealth under a Republican form
of government.
That motion, it may be presumed, is not likely to
be placed before the House by Speaker Abbott,
who will probably declare it to be “ unconstitu-
tional, and in contravention of the “oath of
allegiance to Her Majesty,” to which Rae
subscribed on taking his seat. Speaker Abbott
course, carefully forget to state that
the oath of allegiance ” is a bogus thing,
an enforced (though empty) ceremonial gone
through only under penalty of absolute dis-
franchisement. However, let it be imagined
that Mr. Speaker does not raise the constitutional
spook, and the motion actually is submitted to the
present Assembly. How many of the sitting
members would vote for it ? In the first place
the cowards wouldn’t vote at all, but it is to be
taken for granted that existing machinery could
provide for a call ”of the House and compel
these to assume definite divisional positions.
1 here are many members who would have to vote
straight ” whether they liked it or not—who
would have to vote in harmony with their outside
utterances. For instance, the members for
Northumberland would have to vote—we believe
they would gladly vote —for Rae’s motion.
Avowed Republicans, of whom there are many
in the House, would have to vote for it. The
members for Bourke would have to vote for it
A very large percentage-if not every man jack-
et the Labour-party would have to vote for
it. But the motion would nevertheless be lost
Let it be supposed that this stage had
actually been arrived at. What would Premier
Parkes do? Would he take the judicious and
statesmanlike course of being timely unwell ?
Would he re-break some funny-bone and relegate
his Parliamentary duties to Bruce Smith ? Or
would he, as in duty bound by his “ oath of alle-
giance,” at once rise from his Ministerial seat,
and, seriatim, move that the seat of each rebel
each seditious violator of his “Hoath hof Halle-
giance” be declared vacant? If he did this—
what would follow? A tame submission? Not
in Australia. A dissolution of Parliament? Or
an outside revolution? In either event, the
embryo which now “ is without form and void ”
would vitalise into an entity of discernible pro-
portions and shape and with a self-developing
destiny—the Australian Republic. The Austra-
lian Republic is coming along all the time.

Sweet innocent, take an old man’s advice,
Don’t be wishing for what’s so nice ;
Beware of those lovely girls you meet,
Though the daintiest shoes encase their feet
And the prettiest stockings their legs adorn,
For a rose oftimes conceals a thorn.
They may be everything proper, ’ tis true,
But they probably know too much for you.
Beware of them, and beware of drink ;
Ere you swallow the poison just pause and think.
So go and join a temperance band
And walk with them lovingly hand in hand.
