Soap is one of the most extensively-
advertised articles of commerce, and it strikes the
casual observer as an anomaly that while English
and American manufacturers have actually to
import their tallow from our shores and reshinit
« S s ? a F’ P a yi n g double freightage, they can
still afford to spend large fortunes annually in
advertising their brands and compete and beat
local makers in their own markets. Surely this is a
conundrum the Labour-party might solve but
nonour where honour is due. A local firm are
turning out a soap in tins called “Maefie’s Cold
Water Soap ’’ which is the best and most efficient
soap for washing clothes we have seen tried and
we have no hesitation in recommending its use to
the wearied and worried housewife
* * *
It is a fact worth remembering that Mr
Leon Harris of Market-street, gives the top price
for opals and pearls in the rough. But this is not
all—he also shines as a gem-cutter and diamond-
setter. Any piece of jewellery, no matter how
old-fasmoned it may be, can, under the skilful
touch of Mr. Harris, be re-modelled, so ladies can
keep pace with the fashions of the day, as far as
their jewels are concerned, at a very trifling cost.
The address is Market-street, between Hunter*
Boot Balace and Evening News office.

MAD.
(Special Bulletin Translation.)
TTe died the chief of a high tribunal, an upright
Tndfre whose irreproachable life was known and.
'honoured in every court of France. Advocates,
counsellors, judges, bowed low as a mark of pro-
found respect before his white spare face, lightened
bv those profound and brilliant eyes.
He had passed his life in punishing crime and
nrotecting the weak. The swindler and the raur-
L rer had had no enemy more dreaded ;he seemed
to read in the depths of their souls their most
secret thoughts, and to penetrate at a glance the
whole mystery of their plans.
Now he was dead, at the age of 82, followed by
the homage and regrets of a whole nation. A
vpo-iment of soldiers had escorted him to his tomb,
and men in white ties had poured out over his
coffin lamenting words and tears that seemed
81 Yet this strange paper was found by his notary,
stupefied at the discovery, in the desk where he
had been in the habit of keeping the records of
great criminals. It was headed simply ;
WHY?
June 20, 1851. The trial is over. I have con'
demned Blondel to death. Why did this man
kill those five children? Why? One often meets
men for whom the destruction of life has a certain
voluptuous charm. Yes, it must have a charm
the sweetest of all, perhaps, because is not
killing the nearest approach to creating ? Make
and destroy ! Those two words embrace the
history of the universe, all that is—yes, all. Why
ia it so intoxicating to kill ? ...
June 25.— T0 think that there is a being there,
living, walking, running. A being ? What is a
being? That animated thing, bearing in itself
the principle of movement, and a will that regu-
lates the movement! It belongs to nothing, that
thing. Its feet are not attached to the soil. It
is a grain of life stirring on the earth; and that
crain of life, that comes no one knows whence,
you can destroy as you will. Then it is gone,
nuite gone. It rots, it is no more.
H June 26. —Why, then, is it a crime to kill ? Yes,
why? Oa the contrary, it is a law of nature.
Every being’s mission is to kill; it kills for life’s
sake and it kills for killing’s sake. To kill is in
our nature; we must kill. The beast kills un-
ceasingly, every day, at every instant of its
existence. Man kills unceasingly, for food ; but
as he feels the need to kill for pleasure, too, he
has invented the chase. The child kills the in-
sects he finds, the little birds, all the little living
things that come in his way. But that does not
satisfy the irresistible craving that is in us for
massacre. It is not enough to kill beasts, we are
to kill man. Formerly, this craving
was satisfied by human sacrifices. io-day,
the necessities of social existence have made
murder a crime. The assassin is condemned
and punished. But we cannot live without yield-
ing to this natural and imperious instinct for
death, so we soothe ourselves from time to time
with wars in which one whole nation destroys
another. Then there is a debauch of blood, a
debauch in which the armies run mad, and which
makes drunk even the quiet citizens at home, and
the women and the children as they read under
the lamp at night the glowing descriptions of
slaughter. . . . .
One might think that those whose mission it is
to accomplish these human butcheries would be
despised. No, They are overwhelmed with
honours. They are dressed in gold and bright
scarlet; they wear plumes on their heads, and
decorations on their breasts; crosses, rewards,
titles of all kinds are heaped upon them. Ihey
are proud, respected, loved by women, cheered by
the crowd, solely because their calling is to pour
out human blood. They drag through the streets
their instruments of death, which black-coated
passers-by regard with envy. For to kill is the
great law implanted by nature in the heart of
every being. There is nothing grander or more
honourable than to kill!
June SO. -To kill is a law ; because nature loves
eternal youth. In every unconscious act she
seems to cry “Quick! quick! quick!” ihe
faster she destroys, the faster she renews.
July 2. —A being—what is a being? Every-
thing and nothing. By the mind, it is the reflex
of everything. By memory and knowledge, it is
an epitome of the world, whose history it bears in
itself. A mirror of things and of deeds, every
human being becomes a little universe in the
universe.
But travel; see the nation swarm and seethe,
and man is nothing ! absolutely nothing ! Take
ship, get away from the crowded land, and soon
you see nothing but the shore. The imperceptible
being disappears, so small is he, so insignificant.
Cross Europe in a fast train, and watch from the
window. Men, fur ever men, men innumerable,
unknown, swarming in the fields, swarming in the
streets; dull peasants knowing just enough to
turn the soil; repulsive women knowing just
enough to make soup for their males and bear
children. Go to India, go to China, and you will
see teeming millions of beings who are born, who
live, and who die, leaving no more trace than the
ant crushed on the path. Go to the land of the
negro, squatting in his mud hut; to the land of
the Arab, sheltered under the dusky canvas
flapping in the wind, and you will realise that the
isolated, determinate being is nothing. The race
is everything. What is a single being, this or
that individual of some wandering tribe of the
desert? These races are philosophers; they do
not trouble about death. A man counts for
nothing with them. You kill your enemy ;it is
war. So it was with us in the old days, from
realm to realm, and from province to province.
Yes, travel round the world and see the human
creatures swarm, innumerable and unknown.
Unknown? Ah, that is the solution of the pro-
blem. To kill is a crime, because we have
numbered and marked the individuals. As they
are born, they are registered, named, baptised.
The law claims them. The unregistered being
does not count; kill him on the moor or in the
desert, kill him on the mountain or in the plain,
and what does it matter? Nature loves death,
and she does not punish the slayer.
The real sanctity, then, lies in the registry-
office, It is that which protects a man. The
being is sacred because it is enrolled in the register
—the legal holy of holies!
The State can kill, because it has the right to
revise the register. When it has destroyed two
hundred thousand men in a war, it strikes them
off its list, it wipes them out by the hand of its
clerks. There is an end of it. But we who can’t
make alterations in the public records must res-
pect life. Oh ! Register, glorious divinity who
reignest in the temples of the State, I salute thee.'
Thou art greater than Nature herself.
/ July 3.— There must be a strange, exquisite
pleasure in murder. To have there, before you, a
living, thinking being; to make a little hole in it—
just a little hole—to see the flow of the red some-
thing we call blood, that makes the life—and then
to have nothing before yon but a heap of limp
flesh, cold, inert, empty of thought! .
August 5. I who have passed my life in judg-
ing, in condemning to death by spoken words to
death by the guillotine—those who have put to
death by the knife—l ? if I were to do as the
assassins have done whom I have punished I .
who would know it ?
August 10. —Who would ever know it ? Would
they suspect me, me —especially if I chose a being
in whose removal I could have no interest ?
August 15. —Temptation ! The temptation has
entered into me like a creeping worm. It creeps;
it never rests; through and through my body;
into my mind, that can think only this one thought
—to kill; into my eyes, that ache for the sight of
blood, of death; into my ears, that ring un-
ceasingly with a mysterious something—horrible,
blood-curdling, maddening, like a being’s last cry;
into my legs, that tremble with desire to be gone,
to be gone to the place where if will be; into my
hands, that quiver with the craving to kill. How
sweet it must be, how rare, how worthy of a bold
man, one superior to the common herd, who is
master of his own soul and who seeks refined
sensations !
August 22. I could resist no longer. I have
killed a little animal as an experiment, a com-
mencement. Jean, my servant, had a goldfinch
hanging in a cage at the pantry-window. I sent
Jean on a message, and took the little bird in my
hand. I felt his heart beat. He was warm. I
went upstairs to my room. From time to time I
squeezed him harder; his heart beat faster; it
was cruel and delicious. I nearly choked him.
But then I would not have seen his blood.
I took a pair of scissors, short nail-scissors, and
out his throat in three snips, very gently. He
gasped, and struggled to escape ; but I held him ;
oh ! I held him; I could have held a mad dog.
And I saw the blood flow. How beautiful blood
is, how red and bright and clear! I longed to
drink it. I dipped the end of my tongue in it.
It is good. But he had so little of it, the poor,
little bird. I had not time to enjoy the sight of
it as I should have wished. It must be superb to
watch a bull bleed !
Then I acted as assassins do—real ones. I
washed the scissors, washed my hands, threw out
the water, and carried the body, the corpse, into
the garden to bury it. I buried it under a straw-
berry-plant. It will never be found. Every
morning I shall eat a strawberry from that plant.
Really, you can enjoy life when you know how.
My servant cried over his bird ; he thought it
had escaped. How should he suspect me ?
Ha, ha!
August 25. —I must kill a man. I must.
August SO.— lt is done. What a little thing
it is!
I went for a walk in the wood at Vernes. I
was thinking of nothing—nothing at all. There
was a child on the road, a little boy eating a slice
of bread and butter. He stopped to see me pass
and said—
“ Good morning, M’sieu President.”
The idea came into my head: “ Suppose I kill
him.”
I replied : “You are all alone, my boy ?”
“Yes, sir.”
“All alone in the wood ?”
“Yes, sir.”
The longing to kill him made me drunk as if
with wine. I approached him very gently, con-
vinced that he was going to run away. Then I had
him by the throat—l squeezed it, squeezed with
all my strength ! He stared at me with affrighted
eyes ! What eyes! Round, deep, limpid,
terrible ! Never have I known an emotion so
overpowering—but so short! He clutched my
wrists with his little hands, and his body writhed
like a feather on the fire. Then he moved no
more.
My heart beat, ah ! the bird’s heart! I threw
the body in a ditch, and grass upon it.
I went home and had a good dinner. What a
little thing it is ! At night I was very gay, light-
hearted, almost youthful. I spent the evening
with the prefect. They thought me brilliant.
But I did not see the blood !
August SO. —The corpse has been discovered.
They are hunting for the assassin.
September I. —Two tramps have been arrested.
The evidence is insufficient.
September 2. —The parents came to see me.
They were in tears. Ha, ha!
October 6. —Nothing has been discovered. Some
wandering vagabond must have done the deed.
Ha, ha ! If I had seen the blood flow I think I
should be calm now.
October 10.-—' The desire to kill has got into my
marrow. It is like the fever of love that tortures
you at twenty.
October 20. —Another ! I was walking along the
river bank after breakfast. Under a willow-tree
I saw a fisherman asleep. It was noon. There
was a spade in a neighbouring potato-field which
seemed to have been put there expressly. I went,
for it, and came back. I raised it like a club, and
with one blow of the edge I split open the fisher-
man’s head. Oh, he bled, that fellow! Red
blood, thick with brain ! It ran into the water,
trickling softly. I walked gravely away. If any-
one had seen me ? Ha, ha! I would have made
a splendid assassin.
October 25. —The affair of the fisherman has
made a great stir. The murder is attributed to
the nephew, who was fishing with him.
October 26. —The juge d’instruction* declares
that the nephew is guilty. Everyone in the town
believes it. Ha, ha !
October 27. —The nephew defends himself very
poorly. He had gone to the village to buy bread
and cheese, he says. He swears that his uncle
was killed during his absence ! Who will believe
such a story ?
October 28. —The nephew is so distracted that he
has almost confessed. Ha, ha ! Justice!
November 15. —They have overwhelming evi-
dence against the nephew, who was his uncle’s
heir. I will preside at the trial.
January, 25. —T0 death ! to death ! to death !
I have condemned him to death ! Ha, ha ! The
crown-prosecutor spoke like an angel! Ha, ha !
Another ! I shall go and see him executed !
March, 10. —It is over. They guillotined him
this morning. He is quite dead, quite! It is
delightful! How fine it is to see them slice off
a man’s head ! The blood spurted out in a
stream ! Oh, if I but could, I would have bathed
in it. What an intoxication it would be to lie
there, and get it in my hair and in my face,
and to get up red, all red ! Ah ! if they knew!
Now I shall wait; I can wait. It would take
such a little thing to have me caught.
* * *
The manuscript contained many more pager,
but did not disclose any new crimes. The
scientists to whom it has been confided declare
that there are in the world many unsuspected
madmen, quite as skilful and as formidable as this
demented monster.

Sydney
In an excursion train travelling between
Bundaberg and Brisbane, Q. —upwards of twelve
[ hours distance —the other day, the passengers
were only allowed out of the train —an ordinary
one, unprovided with conveniences—for twenty
minutes during the whole journey. The result is
some lively newspaper correspondence, and, it is
hoped, a lesson to the railway-commissioners.
The best-advertised man in the U.S. just
now is cne Dr. Whitney, of Chicago, who the
other day sewed up a terrible wound in the hand
of a casual patient, a poor man. He demanded
three dollars for the operation, but the man pos-
sessed only two, whereupon Whitney cursed him
and ripped up the wound again with a knife. The
patient fainted outside and was picked up by the
police. From what one reads about Whitney in
the Yankee press from New York right down to
New Orleans it would have been better for
Whitney that he had been lynched.
A gentleman famous as the discoverer of a
certain well-advertised N.Q. mine is now in Syd-
ney. The last time we saw this potentate, who
now appears to feel very well towards himself, was
at a Cloncurry ham-and-cgg banquet, on which
occasion he, assisted by a local celebrity known as
Poker Bill, wound up the festivities by heaving
the crockery and furniture out of the window and
pitching after them the Chinaman who did the
catering for the occasion. The distinguished
Northern visitor will no doubt regard the Sydney
whisky as being very mild after so long a course
of the copper-mine brand dispensed by Father
Lacey, of Cloncurry.
An honest and trustworthy ” young
Chinese in the employ of the Pest and • Telegraph
Department, at Port Darwin, has just confessed
to having stolen the 115 oz. of gold which lately
disappeared in transit. John Chinaman is an A 1
thief. The other day, at Pine Creek, N.T., a
European struck a rich patch of reef gold and
took a couple of buckets of the best out of it for
safety . These he put in his camp and slept along-
side them accompanied by two dogs and armed
with a revolver. In the morning the quartz was
all gone, but the revolver and clogs were in their
places. Not long ago some Chinese were caught
under rather ludicrous circumstances night-
thieving at Pine Creek. Ere going out on prowl
each Chow had put a bell around his neck and
provided himself with hobbles. This explained
how it had been that the European miners used to
lose their best s'one and yet heard no sounds even
if they remained awake all night save the tinkling
of bells and the rattling of the hobble-chains of
What they innocently supposed wore grazing
horses.

There are four Melbourues, the other

In the present unsettled state of the Aus-
tralian legal profession it is interesting to note
that Governor Arthur (Y.D.L.) dismissed his
Attorney-General, J. T. Gellibrand, for unpro-
fessional conduct in drawing pleas for a plaintiff
and afterwards acting against him in court. The
Attorney-General’s conduct was vindicated by the
English bar. It was found that the first counsel
in England often acted against a retaining client
and sometimes drew pleas on both sides.
Dear Bulletin, - Herewith, another anec
dote of the thousand and oneth other great man*
As I remarked before, one ought to perpetuate
memoirs of great men while they are still alive. It
gives them a chance of telling the publisher that
he’s a prosecuting policeman. Here is an aco unit
of my little meeting with the now much-adver-
tised Sir Lepel Griffin. Ho wore a wreath of
smoke from a German china pipe, likewise a
smoking-cap, a velvet coat, and a pair of em-
broidered slippers. In the coolest way he pointed
at me with his pi pe-stem and asked Jim Alexander,
Major R. A., “ Who’s your friend ?” Now, Lepel
(not then Sir Lepel) was in an embroglio with Dr.
Leitner, editor of Public Opinion, published in
Lahore. The sore point was that Leitner had
referred to L.G. as ‘'an oiled Assyrian,” and
them was my sentiments. I could not help hear-
ing his question, so I took it on myself to answer,
“A greasy Babylonian.” Not much fun there,
but those were the only words I ever ad-
dressed to Sir L. G., except the single syllable
“ Done,” when lie offered to back Vedette against
The Cure. But I saw him again at the club table
d’hdte. Peace had been proclaimed, and jolly old
Leitner was proposing the health of Mr. Griffin
when the (alleged! Oiled Assyrian interjected,
“ Let us rather drink to the health of all honest
men; you may sit down, Dr. Leitner.” Of
course, when your health is drunk you are sitting,
but there was a nasty sneer in the tone. The
doctor ran round and slapped the speaker’s
cheek, not very hard, but it was a blow. Then
there was a row, and (I fancy we were winey) I
believe the doctor was off to his bunga’ow to get
his pistols, when ho fell into an open, deep drain
and broke his leg. How the leg an ! the matter
were patched up I never heard. — Hikam A biff.
LITTLE OF THE GUN NOTHING OF
THE DOG.
When Rip Van Winkle awoke from his nap in
the Catskill Mountains in America, lie found him-
self an old man. His dog was dead long ago, and
nothing was left of his gun but the lock and
barrel. And when he went back to the village of
Falling Water, where he used to live, nobody
recognised him. His wife, believing him dead",
had married an innkeeper. Right enough too,
for Rip had lain on the mountain-side, sound
asleep, for twenty years.
A long snooze ; but it seems to me I’d rather be
asleep half my life at a stretch than to stop awake
and be miserable. Yet here is a woman who says,
“ I can truthfully say that for eighteen years I
Was never free from pain for a single day.”
Mercy ! think of that. What a wretched way
to live! Yet I suppose millions of folk crawl
along through the world in that style. Not be-
cause they want to. Heavens, no ! But because
they can’t help it. This was her situation, and
an army of other women (besides crowds of men)
can sympathise with her.
She says : “For over twenty years I was weak
and sickly. At first I had a bad taste in the
mouth, poor appetite, and an uncomfortable feel-
ing at my chest and sides, and often tried to
obtain ease by holding my sides with my hands.
After eating I had grout pain ; it was like a load
at my chest, and I could not bear it until I
vomited all my food up. I would be quite faint
from the want of food but was afraid to cat. At
times I had bad attacks of spasms which nearly
doubled me up, and I rambled up and down the
house for hours together, for I could not even lie
down. Of course I lost a deal of sleep, and in a
morning was so weak and faint I scarcely knew
how to get out of bed and downstairs. For
eighteen years I was never free from pain for a
single day.
“ I saw doctor after do tor, and took a great
quantity of medicine without finding any real
relief. They would not say what was the cause of
my ailment. I was fast wasting away, and did
not think I could live much longer, when one day
in 1881 my husband heard from Mr. Joseph
Cooper, of Bourne, of a medicine called Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup, which he said had cured
him. ‘IfI am spared,’ I said to ray husband, ‘ I
will try it.’ I did so, and before I had taken the
first bottle I found relief, and after taking four
bottles more I was quite well and strong.
“ That is now ten years ago, and 1 have been in
good health ever since, taking just an occasional
dose of the syrup. After my recovery the clergy-
man said to my husband, “Your missis gets
about very different to what she used to do,’and
he told him that Mother Seigel’s Syrup had
wrought the cure. People tell me I look better
than I did twenty years ago, and I feel so strong
that I can now dig potatoes and do work with
anyone, notwithstanding I am 05 years of age.
“My husband suffered terribly from rheuma-
tism and flux, and has found wonderful benefit
from the same medicine. He says he would have
been in the churchyard long ago but for Mother
Seigel’s Syrup. For myself, I can say it saved my
life, and I wish others to know what it has done
for me and mine. (Signed) Mrs. Esther Ayr,
wife of William Ayr, of Thurlby, near Bourne,
Lincolnshire.”
In this brief and simple way Mrs. Ayr tells a
story the full details of which would fill a book.
What hopes and fears ! what hours of keen sorrow
and deep darkness she must have experienced.
Yes, indeed. None can even imagine it except
those who belong to the great Sisterhood of Suffer-
ing. Where she mentions one symptom of her
disease she actually could have named a dozen.
For her malady—the fearfully common and fatal
one, indigestion and dyspepsia—has as many signs
and forms as the mind has fancies, or the heavens
have clouds. From it, as from sin, come a thou-
sand pangs and pains to torment and to crush
helpless humanity. Seeing what her great dis-
covery has accomplished in this and multitudes of
like cases, how good a friend to her kind was
Mother Seigel! Rip Van Winkle awoke from his
nap to find himself old ; Mrs. Ayr awoke from her
long night of illness to find herself young again.
Is not the moral plain enough for all the suffering
millions in England ?

What folly it would be to cut grass with a pair of scissors I Yet peo-
ple do equally silly things every day. Modern progress has grown up
from the hooked sickle to the swinging scythe and thence to the lawn
mower. So don’t use scissors!
But do you use SAPOLIO ? If you don’t you are as much behind the
age as if you cut grass with a dinner knife. Once there were no soaps.
Then one soap served all purposes. Now the sensible folks use one soap
in the toilet, another in the tub, one soap in the stables, and SAPOLIO
for all scouring and house-cleaning.

A Parte Ante.
(Fob The Bulletin.)
The clouds are wild in the western heaven,
The wind blows chill from the sea,
And the wan waves rise, and break, and rave
In endless monotony,
Like the pitiless fate that has touched with fire
My dead past life and me.
And the haggard eyes of the troubled past
Look out from the storm-tossed spray,
On a present as sombre and dull and drear
As the mist on the breast of the bay ;
for I have no faith in the life I live,
No faith in the light of the day.
I have fiercely fought, and ever failed
In the world with its selfish throng,
Where the weak are trodden beneath the heel
Of the tyrannous power of the strong.
Where that which is false is heeded most -
Where right is governed by wrong.
Where in years long gone I sowed the seed
Such a bitter fruit has borne—
A harvest of sadness and sorrow and pain,
Of cherished hopes rudely torn,
Of broken faiths and loves long fled,
And roses that hide the thorn.
And o’er my pain-smitten life there looms
A future all lone and grey,
Which bears in its breast the fruit of sins,
Of the years that have passed away—
Which menaces ever in language harsh,
In W'ords that I cannot say.
Still the distance a golden halo lends,
A wavering shadowy grace.
Shaded with change and a darkened past
And deeds that were wrong and base,
And out from this endless chaos gleams
A woman’s marvellous face.
A radiant face as fearless and fair
As the gleaming summer skies
With the last sweet scent of faded flowers—
From the shadow seems to rise,
With the glittering light upon her hair,
The luminous light in her eyes.
The storm wraok lowers, the sea rolls on,
And breaks in the swelling bay ;
The faith from my heart forever has fled,
What more can 1 do or say ?
For I have no faith in the life I live—
No faith in the light of the day.
Brisbane, Aug. 10.
H. E. P. M'DONALD.
Two years ago an experiment was started
at Thursday Island (Q.) for the production of
pearls by artificial means, and the scheme has
turned out a success. By setting up a process of
complicated irritation the unhappy oyster is made
to grow pearls against his will, which is remark-
ably good for the grower, and remarkably bad for
the oyster. The Bulletin would like that whole-
souled philanthropist, Mr. E. J. H. Knapp, of
Sydney, to look into this matter. Brutality to an
oyster is just .about ten degrees worse than
brutality to a horse, for the oyster can’t kick,
neither can it bolt. The tinned oyster is a tender
and delicate vision, and should be treated with
kindness ; and the oyster stew is a reflective thing
which sits and thinks at the bottom of the sea.
The brutal, sordid speculator who would inflict
life-long suffering on this gentle and soft-voiced
fruit is a degraded wretch and unfit to live—a
being hardened by the love of gain and debased
by greed. Anybody who tries to perpetrate any
such outrage on an oyster in our vicinity must
climb over our dead body to do it.
The Bulletin rises to suggest the urgent
necessity for a reform in the matter of con-
spiracies. It is moved to make this proposal by
the dire monotony of that shearers’insurrectionary
plot about which the Queensland press is still
raving at intervals. The Bulletin doesn’t know
and doesn’t care whether there ever was any plot
at all, but if there was it was a remarkably
-clumsy one, else it would either have succeeded in
one jump, or it would have failed quietly, and no
one would ever have heard anything about it.
The amateur conspirator, in fact, is a failure, and
he can’t conspire worth a rap. Therefore it has
occurred to us that there should be a good opening
for a limited liability company which would
always keep on hand a staff of trained and
efficient conspirators, and conduct nefarious
plots on a fixed percentage. The chief essentials
for the business are a stock of dark lanterns and
slouched hats, and a capacity for making signals
in cypher and hovering mysteriously at obscure
corners, and interring compromising documents in
cellars at midnight; and the company would under-
take all these branches of the business and conduct
them in a way that would give general satisfaction.
Corners would be mysteriously hovered at with
punctuality and industry, and at the lowest
current rates, and two or more corners would be
hovered at on reduced terms. In the case of
anybody who wanted to get up a conspiracy on
the plan adopted by the lamented G. Fawkes, the
company would undertake to stand in a cellar and
look gloomily at a stack of coals if required. A
quantity of collars would be “stood in” at cut
rates. Treasonable telegrams would be despatched
and received, and compromising documents buried
secretly at midnight and afterwards discovered
through the agency of the company’s own base
deceiver. Base deceivers would be supplied at a
surprising reduction on all previous quotations.
Oaths would be administered, and bones found,
also dark alleys and ruined buildings lurked in
suspiciously. Owing to its long experience
of the trade, the company is convinced
that it could lurk in a way that no
private firm could compete with, and look
more suspicious than anybody ever previously
heard of. Dynamite would be heaved, at a mere
shade over cost price, at the company’s own im-
perial tyrant, who would be always at the office
from nine till four—on Saturdays nine till one;
republics proclaimed by private arrangement ;
seditious pamphlets always kept in stock, and the
characters of the minions of despotism blasted by
contract. If necessary, Freedom would shriek
when Kosciuscko fell Kosciuscko always on
hand. Insurrections got up, either with or with-
out bloodstained massacre, at the shortest notice.
The cadaverous office patriot would languish on a
straw bed in a dungeon after the conspiracy
burst, and utter moral sentiments through the
window to the weeping multitude and make them
groan at the bloated autocrat. Bloated autocrat
furnished on shortest notice. Terms, spot cash or
marked cheque.

During the height of the Palmer rush
enormous prices were charged for supplies, and
the queerest tricks were resorted to for the pur-
pose of quitting certain goods for which there
proved to be no demand. One of the first store-
keepers to arrive brought as part of his stock a
large quantity of horse-shoe nails and rubbed his
hands in delight when he heard that his was the
only supply on the field. However, there was no
demand for the nails, and the clever speculator
found himself “had.” The miners got wind of
the fact and rubbed their hands in the joyful
belief that for once the biter had been bitten, and
so every man who visited the store asked in a
casual, satirical sort of way if the boss “ had any
nails ” ? But Shylock grinned grimly and
answered not. By and bye the storekeeper’s
opportunity came witli what threatened to be a
Hour-famine. He had the only stock of flour, and
calmly announced that, pending further arrange-
ments, all persons buying at his establishment
would have to take a pound of horse shoe
nails with eacli and every 10 pounds of flour.
Loud were the curses, but the men could
not help themselves. In fact, they were “had”
over and over again. They couldn’t see the
force of humping horse-shoe nails over the‘stony
ridges between the store and their camps ; so, as
each man left the store he would throw away his
thare of the nails, and then the nigger hired for
that purpose by the boss, would gather them up
and have them in readiness for the next applicant
for damper-foundation with horse-nail trimmings.
The scheme worked for a while, only some of the
men got suspicions, and finally comprehending the
sell they induced the merchant to administer a
dose of nails to an abandoned shaft, said dose
consisting of all his remaining stock.
The same old story. At Brighton (Eng.)
the other day John Muspratt, a Cambridge
graduate in divinity, got into trouble for brutally
assaulting and threatening to murder a girl who
had refused to marry him. He was released on
promising “ to go to Australia and not return for
three years.” The British beak is always ready
to let off the British blackguard if he will promise
to go to Australia, and, under our grand old Con-
stitution, there is no means by which the said
blackguard can be kept out. The procession keeps
on coming with never a stoppage. The advance
guard consists of a great array of cancer patients,
and consumptives, and individuals with livers
all awry and diseased kidneys. Then behind them
marches, a hundred or so abreast, the vast army
of expatriated drunkards with b'eared eyes and
shattered hats, arid the big battalions of bounced
lunatics, who have been shunted just ere their
disease became too conspicuous. Still they come.
Then tin re is a rolling tide of wife-beaters,
forgers, small embezzlers, brutal-assault men, and
all the miscellaneous gang who were short in their
cash, and who undertook to go to good old Austra-
lia instead of going to gaol. And at the tail-end of
the horde come the phalanx of assorted bigamists,
the Sunday-school snufflers who had led the choir
astray, the escaped pastors who have left with the
trust funds, and the Irish informer with a tumour
in his stomach. They are a gaudy crowd, all
loafers, all bent on looking after the ‘‘silken
bonds,” and all dreadfully in need of a cwt. of
pig-iron as a sinker, and then a drop into the
fathomless sea.
The Marriage of Metals.
A MARVELLOUS SAVING OF LABOUR AND TlMv
SILENCE AND SPEED A SPECIALTY. *“
A MACHINE THAT WELDS IRON AND BRASS
THE ELECTRIC WELDING COMPANY. ’
The welding machine can be seen daily at th.
Exhibition Buildings, Melbourne, Saturday!
excepted. It is in charge of Mr. Thomas T
Hraper, who is explaining its working on behalf
of the Electric Welding Company.
It has attracted a great deal of attention from
practical men, real ironworkers, who gain thpL
living by the sort of work they were witnessing
and their verdict is unanimous— summed un
one word—marvellous. 1 1
To the ordinary layman, the average represents,
ti ve of the intelligent public, it was a silent puzzle
It performed such unquestionably clever wort
with so little of the bustle and noise that aecom-
panics the old-fashioned method of the smithy
and only taking a few seconds where 15 minute
had been requisite.
The welding is accomplished without noise or
hammering. There was no bier anvil and rust
strewn floor, no smoky smithy with ponderous
bellows and bed of coal; but merely a machine
which might have been a sewing machine, and
which was tended by a young man in the guise of
a clerk m a fashionable suit of clothes, with a
pendant watch chain. There was an absence of
the muscular village blacksmith, and his leathern
apron and the flying sparks. The whole apparatus
might have been located in a lady’s draw ing-rooni
and even the conversation need not have been
interrupted.
Lying about were samples of the work done •
steel, iron, brass, even lead, welded together, and
the most difficult problems solved satisfactorily
Tor instance, one of the jobs which have long been
desirable, but regarded as impracticable, has been
the joining of fractured tram-cables. Tins has
now been accomplished by the electric welder.
Hitherto when a cable parted the only way of
joining was by the splice, which, however neatly
performed, was necessarily cumbrous. Now the
process is so simple that a child could do it. It i s
only necessary to saw off the ends evenly, brino-
the pieces together, and turn a switch. The
electric current is responsible for the repairs. For
a second or so, possibly 10, the wire ends for the
distance of an inch on each side of the joint in-
crease in brilliancy till they glow'. A lever is
pulled meanwhile, so as to effect the union, and
the weld is complete with the turning off of the
current.
Nothing could be simpler to look at, nothing
more interesting; and to the expert, accustomed to
old methods, it is little short of miraculous. The
principle involved in the new art is that of
causing currents of electricity to pass through the
abutting ends of the pieces of metal which are to
be welded, thereby generating heat at the point of
contact, which also becomes the point of greatest
resistance, while at the same time mechanical
pressure is applied to force the parts together,
with the advantage that the interior of the joint
T! a f efficiently united as the visible exterior.
With such a method and apparatus, it has been
found possible to accomplish not only the common
kinds of welding of iron and steel, but also of
metals which have heretofore resisted attempts at
welding, and have had to be be brazed or
soldered. As regards strength it has beeli
demonstrated that the weld is the last point
at which the iron breaks under the severest tests.
The variety of uses to which this method
applies is almost endless, ranging from the
welding of heavy cables to jewellers’ chains, and
being suitable lor soldering, brazing, shaping,
forging, riveting, and bending of metals, no
matter of what kind. The machines of the
Electric Welding Company possess the character-
istics of simplicity of instruction and adapta-
bility to an almost eu ft® list of requirements in
the metal-working business, including even the
tempering of springs and the welding of dissimilar
metals. Like many other new inventions, it
comes from America, and the success which its
production has met with oyer in the United States
bids fair to be repeated in Australasia, judging
from the favourable criticisms of those most
capable of judging, the practical iromvorkers who
have examined its operations in Melbourne.
As regards the commercial side of this electric
welder, it is proposed to supply the machines to
the trade on lease, merely charging for the work
actually performed, as in addition to its other
ingenious qualities it automatically registers the
number of welds performed by it.
The prospectus will be shortly issued, and there
appears to be a successful future for the opera-
tions of the company.

for price-list of their goods for gentlemen’s wear ;
also for self-measurement forms and patterns
from the Tailoring Department.

To H. P. Kugelmann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but 1 became worse every day, wasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
chd of three months I was taken had one morning, and
yomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th dav of December, 1888.
\ Peter Smytiib, J.P.
